eternal life freely to be given by and for Christ. And generally, whatsoever
confirms another thing, ought not forthwith to be counted the cause of it.

to be elected according to the foreknowledge of God the father. 1. Peter 1.
Acts 15.
when as yet we were not, chose us according to his foreknowledge, whereby he
knows all his future works, and has disposed them from eternity.

We are elected in Christ, says the Apostle, Ephesians. 1. Therefore we are not
elected by any absolute decree, or mercy of God, but limited and described in
Christ. absolute. The decree of election how far absolute or not.
The decree and mercy of God, according to which he chooses some out of
mankind that shall certainly be delivered, may be called absolute, as touching
the cause, as far forth as nothing inherent in the elect themselves, can be
the cause of either the decree or mercy: (against which the opinion of the
Pelagians and Semipelagians, did set the foreseeing of works, or of faith
also, or unbelief:) but in the respect of the means, whereby he appointed to
save the elect in mercy, that mercy is wholly limited in Christ the Mediator,
by whom God decreed to save, whosoever are saved. To be chosen in Christ, what
it is.
Therefore the meaning is: He has chosen us in Christ, not as being in him, or
would be afterward as of ourselves, but that we might be in him, and by him
might be saved. And so Paul himself expounds: He has predestined us, that
he might adopt us for sons through Christ Jesus, and that according to the good
pleasure of his will. And this very thing is also in force concerning
reprobation: that God neither destroys nor has purposed to destroy the
reprobates by absolute justice, to wit, without their desert: but whom he
destroys, he destroys, and has purposed from everlasting to destroy for
sins.

The sixth objection.
That which is the cause of justification, the same is of Election: and that
which is the cause of damnation, is of reprobation: But the cause of
justification is faith concurring with the mercy of God and apprehending it:
and the cause of damnation is unbelief despising the grace of God offered in
Christ, and other sins, Therefore etc. Mayor. They that be justified be
elected surely, so that we may in judging by that which is later, determine,
that doubtless they are elected, who by faith lay hold upon the mercy
promised for Christ's sake, and do not cast away that confidence unto the end.
But it neither ought nor can be granted that the next cause of justification
and election is one and the same (such as faith is in the matter of
justification.) For justification is the effect of election, as even says it
self, whereby we are justified, according to the saying: Whom he has
predestined, them he has called, Romans 8. Acts 13.
whom he has called, them also he justified. Again, As many as were
foreordained to eternal life, believed. And now it is manifest, that the cause
of the cause, that is, election, is also the cause of the effect, or the thing
caused, (as they do say,) to wit, of justification, but not contrariwise:
because one and the same thing should be the cause of it self. Wherefore the
very effects of election may be so ordered, that one is the cause of another,
as faith of justification, justification of glorification: but no effect of
election can be considered as the cause thereof; and by consequent faith,
seeing it is the effect of election, cannot be thought to be the cause of it as
well as of justification. The effect of Reprobation is Gods forsaking after
which follow unbelief and other sins. The 7. objection.
The same we may judge of unbelief and other sins which albeit properly
they are not to be called the effects of reprobation, yet the effect thereof is
Gods forsaking, after which unbelief and other sins do follow.

This whole answer is confirmed by those things, which before in the 11.
chapter are recited out of Luther concerning the Jews, that were through
unbelief cut from the Olive tree, and the Gentiles grafted in the same by
faith.

The seventh objection is like unto this. We must judge of Predestination
neither by reason, nor by the law, but by the Gospel: But the Gospel witnesses
that the believers are received in to grace, and saved, and thereby are elect:
and on the contrary, that unbelievers and such as continue in sins are
damned, and thereby reprobates. Election therefore and reprobation depend on
faith or unbelief of men. Minor affirms nothing of the cause of
predestination, but shows only this, that by the mark of faith or final
unbelief, the elect or reprobates are discerned. Whereupon this only
follows, that we must judge also according to this testimony of the Gospel,
who be elected, who rejected. In the mean while the Gospel does not deny,
but manifestly affirms, that faith, obedience, perseverance, are the free
gifts of God, and are given, or not given of him, to whom it pleases God
according to his good pleasure: Mat. 11. verse. 25 and 13.11. John 6. verse.
44.45. and 65. and 10 verse. 26. Ephe. 1.8.15. and the rest. 2. Tim. 1.9. Heb.
8.10. Ier. 31. etc. Hence in judging according to the Gospel, A Syllogism
proving by the Gospel, that God has decreed to save some and forsake others,
only of his good pleasure and will.
we shall gather (as the Gospel teaches a) man to be saved or not saved: so
God has appointed from everlasting to save or not to save him. But the Gospel
not only teaches that a man is saved by faith, and does perish through
unbelief, but also teaches that even faith and other benefits, by which as
by means man comes to salvation, are given to some and not given to others
of God, even as pleases him. Therefore even faith and such kind of benefits
unto salvation, God has decreed from everlasting to give to some men, and not
to give to others, even as it pleased him.


CHAP. XIII. A wicked eye without cause suspecteth God of injustice.
The reasons of the objection.
and refuse another of his mere will, for it seems unjust in distributions,
if we distribute unequally to them that be equal. But men, if we set aside
the difference of merits, are equal. Therefore if without difference of
merits, God does unequally distribute, choosing one, and refusing another, it
seems that there is unrighteousness with him. Further how shall we defend the
righteousness of God, if of his mere will he has predestined so many
thousands of men, not moved with any merit or work of theirs, unto eternal
torments. This seems unjust, cruel, and intolerable to judge of God, and
herewith so many and great men in so many ages have been offended, and who
would not be offended, says Luther, De ser. arbit. Answer to the first reason
of the objection.
when he had largely answered this very objection. Concerning inequality,
already before I have answered, that it appertains to justice, in those
things that be distributed of duty, that unequal things be not distributed to
them that be equal: but not in those things, which a man of his own accord
and of favor distributeth without any injury of another. As that householder
reproved them that murmured: Friend, I do thee no wrong, take that thine is,
and go thy way. I will give to this last, as unto thee. May I not do what I
will with mine own? Is thine eye evil because I am good? Let them therefore
learn to restrain that wicked eye, who murmur against God as unjust, and an
acceptor of persons.

August. epist. 105.
Therefore Let us (as Augustine godly advises us) give thanks to our Savior,
seeing that we see not that rendered unto us, that we know, in the damnation of
the^ that are like us, to have bin also due unto us. Neither let us be
unthankful, De bono persevere. cap. 8.
that our merciful God (according to his good pleasure, to the praise of his
glorious grace) has freed so many from so deserved destruction, that if he
should deliver none from thence yet he should not be unjust.

For on whom there is no mercy showed, Enchirid. ad Laur. cap. 99.
them God judges, he deals not unjustly with them. And as undeserved grace
is given to such as are freed: so no other but due punishment is rendered to
them that are damned, that neither they can boast themselves to be worthy,
nor these complain that they are unworthy. Epist. 105.
For because the whole lump is worthily condemned, justice rendereth due
dishonor, and grace bestows due honor, not through the prerogative of
desert, nor by necessity of destiny, nor by rash fortune, but through the
depth of the riches of the wisdom and knowledge of God. De bono perseu. cap.
8. epist. 106.
If therefore Gods goodness be understood in forgiving debt, and his equity in
requiring it, there is no way iniquity found in God. He commends mercy that
is set free, and he that is punished blames not judgment.

Do praedest. and great. cap. vlt.
I will say somewhat more with Augustine. If mankind, that at the first was
created of nothing, should be borne without the due original of death and
sin, and yet the omnipotent creator would for ever condemn some of them to
destruction: who could say to the Almighty Creator, why have thou done thus?
For he that freely gave them their being, when they were not, had power to
appoint to what end they should be. Neither could the rest say, why should Gods
will vary, all men's merits being alike? because the potter has power of the
clay. But now seeing just punishment is rendered to such as shall be damned,
and undeserved grace is bestowed upon such as be saved; what man forgetting
man's condition, may discuss the secrets of Gods mind?

Ad Simpl. lib. 1. q. 2.
or forgiueth the debt. Hereupon Augustine: In Adam we all die, and we are one
lump of sin, owing punishment to the divine and high justice, which whether it
be exacted or pardoned, there is no unrighteousness. And it is an arrogant
part in debtors to judge, of whom it is to be required, and to whom it is to
be remitted. It belongs to God to require it of whom it pleases him, and to
forgive it to whom it pleases him, who does not require that which is not due,
neither forgiueth that which is none of his. The same writer elsewhere: Epist.
105.
Why God does thus to one man, and otherwise to another, his ways are
unsearchable, and his judgments past finding out: the great depth whereof we
ought rather to reverence, than curiously to search out, that we may escape a
dangerous downfall.

Therefore repressing the impudency of this question, let us say with the
Apostle: Romans 11.
O the depth of the wisdom and knowledge of God, how unsearchable are his
judgments, How the mouths of wicked men must be stopped about this doctrine.
and his ways past finding out? Also: O man, who art thou that reasonest with
God? Does the pot say to the potter, why have thou made me thus? Has not the
potter power over the clay? Neither let it grieve us thus to stop the mouths
of exclaimers. For if they be not ashamed to gainsay not us, but Paul; why
should it irke us to tell them this again and again, that the Apostle has
said? De verbis Apost. sermon. 20.
Augustine notably following the Apostle says: Thou man, expectest from me an
answer, and I am a man. Therefore let us both hear him that says: O man who
art thou? Better is faithful ignorance, than indiscreet knowledge. Seek for
deserts, thou shall find nothing but punishment. O height. Peter denies the
thief believes. Mark this notable saying.
O the depth. Seek thou a reason? I will be abashed at the depth. Reason
thou, I will marvel. Dispute thou, I will believe. I see the depth, I cannot
reach to the bottom. Paul rested because he found a thing to marvel at. He
calls them judgments unsearchable and art thou come to seek them out?
Again, Sermon. 254. de Temp.
I confess the counsel of God as a man, I cannot declare it. For with the
Apostle Paul I know how to be astonished: O the depth of his wisdom and
knowledge. To us belong consideration admiration trembling and exclamation,
because we cannot pearce into them: but unto him what? glory for ever and ever,
whether it be concerning the vessels for honor, or vessels for dishonor.

And in this place we must be admonished, that in the things that have been
spoken, Ad ^an^. ca. 99.
there is great reason rendered against certain foolish men (whom the
forenamed author often nippeth) who think, that the Apostle failed in his
answer, and through want of rendering a reason repressed the boldness of the
gainsaier. For we are called back to the consideration of our capacity, in
brief words surely, but such as bring great weight by aggravating of names,
when he says: O man, who art thou that reasonest with God? man is opposed to
God, and the clay to the potter. Likewise in that with trembling he cries out:
O the depth, how unsearchable are the judgments of God, he sufficiently
declares, that these works of God may be secret, but cannot be unjust:
because they be his judgments, who is just, and judges the world, and whose
will is so the chiefest rule of justice, Gods justice unlike to man's
that what thing soever he wills, and does, must therefore be counted just,
because he does it. For it is not lawful to dispute of the divine justice
according to man's justice, or rather man's pride, that has the name of justice.
De seruo arbit. circa finem.
For as Luther notably proves: If Gods justice were such, as could be judged
to be just by man's capacity, it were not divine, and it should nothing differ
from man's justice. Further, seeing God is wholly incomprehensible, and
inaccessable by man's reason, (for what is man compared to God? what is our
power, knowledge, substance, and all that we have to God?) it is meet, yea
necessary, that his justice also be incomprehensible.

De praedest. and gra. cap. 2.
Wherefore Augustine not without cause says: Who is so mad, or rather, who is
such a blasphemer to say that we must dispute of the justice of God by the
rule of man's justice? which doubtless is an enemy to Gods justice, We must
judge soberly and reverently of the works of God.
and is unjust. It is manifest, that every thing that is howsoever just,
flows from him who is most just. Who then shall he be, that hangs the
wisdom of God (that abides unchangeable, creates governs, and preserves
all things that be) upon the pleasure of man's wisdom? We must not therefore
reason as vain man thinks best, of that majesty of the divine wisdom. God
must be adored, Lib. 2. contra Marc.
and not judged, says Tertullian, sharply reproving the controllers of the
Deity: saying, Thus God ought not to be used, and so rather he ought to be.
But the foolish things of God are wiser than men. And then especially is he
great, when to man he is small: and most just, when to man he is unjust, says
he.

But say they, all these things are true, and sufficient to defend the justice
of Gods election and reprobation, The adversaries defend Gods justice in
election and reprobation by presupposing of man's fall.
if we consider how God deals with us by presupposing the fall of mankind.
For whereas we were all inthralled to eternal damnation, he can be charged
with no injustice for delivering some from thence mercifully, and punishing
others justly, as pleases him, because he may have mercy on whom he will, and
at his pleasure punish whom he will. How the flesh concludes thereof to excuse
it self.
But without the will of God we had not fallen into that corruption, wherinto
Adam carried all his posterity headlong with himself. Why then is he yet
angry? why does he lay to men's fault, and punish in them those things, that he
has necessarily imposed upon them by his own ordinance? For who may resist
his will? Thus flesh concludes, to excuse itself, and to lay the fault in
God. And it is no marvel, for when Adam was corrupted, after whose image we
are borne, he did the same thing in Paradise. But faith and the spirit judge
far otherwise.

1. We may here retort again that of the Apostle: O man, The judgment of
faith and the spirit,
who art thou that reasonest with God? Does the pot say to the potter, why have
thou made me thus?

2. All Christians confess, that for the fall of our first parents in
Paradise, sin and death not only temporal, but also much more eternal, have
justly run over all men. And against the prophanenes of the wicked, the Lord
himself will maintain sufficiently his own justice by making them to
condemn themselves. Romans 9.
Why contend we then of his injustice, seeing his justice is confessed?

3. Man doubtless fell through his own fault, and not Gods. The distinction of
will and permission.
Which thing some going about to expound, fly unto the distinction of will and
permission: as though mankind fell in Adam, God barely only permitting it,
and not after any sort also willing it, without whose will yet, not so much as
a sparrow falls upon the ground. The very word of permission I do not
reject, which the Scripture also uses: Matthew. 10.
but I find fault with the expounding of it: because God permits not
unwilling but willing, whatsoever he permits. After another manner therefore,
and that true and sound, does Augustine show, De Correp. and gra. cap. 11.
how man fell through his own fault, and not Gods: to wit, God, who made all
things very good, had given man a good will, How Adam fell through his own
fault, and not Gods.
wherein he had made him righteous, and after his own image: he had given him
also a help, whereby he might continue in that image, if he would. And that
he might be willing or unwilling hereto, he left it in his own power.
Therefore the cause was in man himself, and in the devil, that he was made
worse. In the devil, because he persuaded: in man, because he with a free will
consented, and so through freewill forsaking God, he found the just judgment
of God, that with his whole posterity, which as yet being in his loins wholly
had sinned, he should be damned.

Why God suffered Adam to fall. Enchir. cap. 37. De Correp, and gra. cap. 18.
And whereas it was not given unto him, to continue in that upright and faultles
state, doubtless therefore it was not given, because God would not: judging it
to be a better thing to do good out of evil, than to permit no evil to be,
as Augustine says. Which thing in another place notably expounding, he
writes: We profitably confess, what we rightly believe, that God, and the
Lord of all things, who created all things exceeding good, and foreknew that
evil would arise out of good, and knew that it more appertained to his
almighty goodness, even out of evil to do good, than not to suffer evil to
be; had so ordained the life of Angels and men, that therein he would show
first, what their freewill was able to do, and then what the benefit of his
grace, and the judgment of his justice could bring to pass. Of this thing see
also Tertullian lib. 2. contra Marcio. and 2. Sent. distinct. 23. why God
suffered man to be tempted, knowing that he would fall. And lib. 1. did. 45.
it is learnedly declared, how and how far forth Gods permission must be
referred to his will, according to that of Augustine: Enchir. cap. 95. M^^ke
how God wills good and evil things,
Nothing is done, unless the Almighty would have it to be done, either by
suffering it to be done, or by doing it himself. Where he includes all good
and evil things that are done, but with this difference, that he be
understood to will evil, by suffering it to be done: and to will good, by
doing it himself. For he suffers doubtless not unwilling, but willing, as the
same Augustine says. And de praedest. and gra. cap. 15. Enchir. ad (...). cap.
100.
he says, that all things are either done, the Lord assisting, or else
permitted, the Lord forsaking them, that yet we may know, Nothing is done
against the Lords will, and why.
that nothing at all is done against the Lords will. Certainly if any thing be
done, that God simply and every way will not have done, or else if that be not
done, that he wills to be done, the very beginning of our faith is in hazard,
wherein we confess that we believe in God almighty, and some God is brought
in out of Epicurus his school. Psalm. 105
For our God does in heaven and earth whatsoever pleases him.

Luther of this whole matter speaks thus: Lib. de ser. arbit. cap. 152.
To them that inquire why he permitted Adam to fall, when he was able to save
him, it is said; It is God, of whose will there is no cause nor reason. See how
he includes permission under his will. Whereupon also chap. 197. he writes:
Whether God suffer, or else incline a man, that suffering or inclining comes
not to pass, but by Gods will because the will of man cannot avoid the work
of almighty God.

CHAP. XIIII. Of the effects of Election.
HEreafter now we must entreat of the effects both of election and also of
reprobation. And because the predestination of Saints, which we call election,
Election what it is.
is a preparation of grace, that is, of glory hereafter, and of benefits in
this world, whereby as by means the elect are lead to the glory appointed for
them: both, the end, and the means, The effects of it be the end and means.
The end double. Rom 9.23. Ephesians. 1.7. The means be al benefits, and they be
of two sorts.
be effects of election. By the end we mean salvation and the glory of the
elect. For in respect of them, that is the end of election, albeit in respect
of God there is another, and higher, to wit, the demonstration of his rich
grace in the vessels of mercy to his glory. As for the means that be
subordinate to this end, they be all benefits whatsoever, whereby whosoever are
delivered, are most certainly set at liberty, as Augustine says, de bono
perseu. cap. 14. And these be of two sorts altogether: some are necessary to
the common salvation of all, Some necessary to the common salvation of all men
and infants. Some peculiar to men only.
men grown and infants: as for example, the merit of Christ, justification,
and regeneration by the holy Ghost. Some do follow men grown only, through
the want of discretion of good and evil in children, as is the knowledge of
Christ, a true confidence in him, the study of good works, perseverance in
temptations, and such like.

Four principal effects of election.
And although there be many and sundry effects of election: yet such as be
more special whereunto other commonly are reduced be four: to wit, Christ as
the Mediator with the whole work of his humiliation vocation effectual unto
Christ justification glorification. Hereof comes that truly golden chain of
the Apostle, Romans 8.
that whom God has predestined, them he calls: and whom he calls
(understand that calling that is according to Gods purpose) them also he
justifies: and whom he justifies, them he also glorifieth. And of Christ
without whom no man can be saved he straightway adds: What then shall we say
to these things? If God be for us, who can be against us* who has not spared
his own son, but given him for us all. Who shall lay any thing to the charge
of Gods elect? Who is he that can condemn? It is Christ, who is dead yea who
is risen again; who is at the right hand of God, who makes also requests for
us.

The 1. effect of election, Christ.
That we may therefore speak something of these beginning at the Mediator and
head of the elect, our Lord Jesus Christ: he surely is the only foundation of
our conjunction with God, and therefore of all our blessedness: for we had not
been capable of so great glory, whereunto we are elected, unless our heavenly
father turning his eyes from our unworthiness upon Christ, had made us
acceptable unto himself in that his beloved. Therefore Paul witnesses, that
we were elected in Christ before the foundations of the world were laid:
Ephesians. 1.
that is, (as himself interprets) that we were predestinate to be adopted
for sons, and to obtain other heavenly good things by and for Christ. In
which sense also he writes to Timothy: 2. Tim. 1.
that grace was given us in Christ Jesus before the worlds: ^s if he should say,
that God from eternity decreed to give us grace, whereby we are saved, but in
Christ, the fountain of grace.

Thus then let us determine and judge of a certainty, that God (when he
minded to have mercy upon some, that he might make known the riches of his
glory toward the vessels of mercy, and had need also of a fit Mediator
hereunto, who might by his death and satisfaction pacify the wrath of God, and
procure for them righteousness and life that was lost, and might defend and
maintain salvation obtained) ordained by his eternal and very fatherly
counsel, that his son (of like substance and eternity with the father) in
the fulness of time assuming truly man's nature, should dye for our sins, and
rising again from the dead, should reign at the right hand of God, and make
intercession for us. So Christ, as he is the Mediator, is the first and
principal effect of Gods predestination, 1. Pet. 1.
from whom all other things flow: as Peter also testifies of that immaculate
and undefiled Lamb, (by whose precious blood we are redeemed) that he was made
manifest in the last times for the faithful sake, being fore ordained before
the foundations of the world were laid. Notably Augustine: De praedest. sanct.
cap. 15.
He being one was predestined to be our head: we being many, were
predestinate to be his members.

Hereby also appears, The proper cause of Christ's sending and suffering, was
the salvation of the elect only.
that ye proper cause of the sending and suffering of the son of God, was the
salvation of the elect, and therefore as touching the purpose of God, and the
effect of the sending and suffering of Christ, that he was properly sent, and
suffered only for the elect. For unless he had been ordained a Mediator for
the elect, there was no cause why he should come in the flesh, much less, why
he should expose himself to the most shameful death of all.

An other effect of election as we said is vocation, Vocation, the second
effect of election. Romans 8. 2. Tim. 1.
whereby the predestination of a man now begins to be fulfilled and made
manifest. For whom he has predestined, he calls, says Paul. And
elsewhere: He has called us with an holy calling, not according to our works,
but according to his own purpose and grace, Vocation twofolde.
which was given us in Christ before the world. Where he plainly sets down
vocation to be the effect of the giving of grace from everlasting, that is, of
predestination. But that it may be understood, what and what manner of calling
that is, we must distinguish between external and internal calling. External
vocation twofold Natural. Psalm. 19.
Again the external is there natural or else supernatural. That is to be
understood of the former, which the Psalmist sings: The heavens declare the
glory of God, and the firmament shows his handy work. There is neither
speech nor language, Romans 2.
where their voice is not heard. And Paul writes to the Romans: that the
invisible things of God, to wit, both his eternal power, and also deity, are
perceived by the creation of the world, Acts 14.
to this end that men may be made without excuse. And elsewhere: he left not
himself without witness, by doing good, and giving from heaven fruitful
seasons.

This testimony of nature never cease to declare the goodness and power of
the Lord, but by perpetual preaching shows the majesty of the maker of it.
Supernatural.
Yet because our ears are deaf at the voice of nature, so that we do not
hear profitablie the things we be admonished of; ^he doctrine of the law and
the Gospel has been added, and ministers of the word and grace have been
instituted, by whom in season and out of season faith and repentance are
preached (as it falls out,) to all, elect and reprobates: seeing they are
mingled one with another, and cannot, nor ought to be severed by the judgment
of men: Matthew 22.
The Lord speaks of this calling, that many are called, but few are chosen.
For all obey not the Gospel, Esaias 53. and 65. Romans 10. The inward calling.
that hear it. For Esaias says: Lord who has beleeed our word? all the day
long I have stretched forth my hands to a rebellious and gain saying people.
Therefore the inward and effectual calling unto Christ, remains, which is
proper to the elect, To whom it belongs. Romans 8.
which Paul terms a calling according to the purpose of God. We know, (says
he) that to such as love God, all things work for the best even to such, as be
called according to And this calling is nothing else than a certain divine
inspiration of grace, What it is.
whereby a man is moved to assent unto, and obey the Gospel, that is, to
believe and repent. And this vocation is made partly by the word, How it is done
partly and principally When men are thus called. Matthew. ^0. August. de
praedest. sanc. cap. 3.
some at the eleventh hour of the day, as it pleases God to dispense in them
his grace. The description propounded, Augustine confirms, where he largely
handles this matter. That vocation (says he) according to the purpose of God,
belongs not to all that be called, Cap. 16.
but to the elect only. These God calls, to make them members of his son,
not by that calling, whereby they were called, that refused to come to the
marriage, but by that calling whereby a believer is made. Unto which calling who
so appertain, Cap. ^.
they be all taught of God, and none of them can say: I have believed, that I
might be thus called: for the mercy of God has prevented him, whereby he is
so called, that he might believe. For all that be taught of God, come to the
son, because they have heard and learned of the father. This school is far
removed from the understanding of the flesh, wherein the father is heard, and
teaches, that men may come to the son: neither deals he with the care of
the flesh, but of the heart.

Hereupon surely, when the Gospel is preached, Ibid. Aug. Mark this.
some believe not, yea gainesay it. For they that believe the preacher outwadly
speaking, within hear of the father and learn: but they that believe not,
outwardly hear, but within do not hear nor learn, that is, it is given to
them to believe, Ioh. 6.
and is not given to the other: because no man (says he) comes unto me
unless the father, that sent me, draw him. Which thing afterward is spoken
more plainly. No man comes unto me, To be drawn what it is.
except it be given him of the father. Therefore to be drawn of the father to
Christ, as also to hear and learn of the father, that a man may come to
Christ, is nothing else than to receive a gift of the father, whereby he may
believe in Christ. These are the words of Augustine the chief of the soundest
fathers.

But here we must beware of a fouresould error: Four errors. The first of the
Pelagians.
first of the Pelagians, whereby they aduauncing themselves against grace, and
setting their face against heaven, said: that a man has such strength, that as
of himself he is able to believe, and to work well so that the will of God be
preached, Aug. ad Quod vuli Deum, haer. 88. Faith and repentance are wrought in
men by God. Ioh. 6.
and hereto only he is helped of God by the law and the doctrine of the Gospel,
that he may learn what things he ought to do and hope for. So according to
their opinion, that should only be the calling of God, which is outwardly made
by the word. As though he did discern such as hear the Gospel from such as
bear not, and not rather the believers from such as believe not, who said: No
man comes unto me, unless it be given him of the father. And in many places
the Scripture teaches, that faith and conversion, and what good work soever
we have, flow from God. Lament. 5.
Turn us unto thee O Lord and we shall be turned, says Jeremy. Ierem. 31.
Eze. 36.
And it is promised in the Prophets, that the time shall come, when God will
write his law in their hearts, and give them a new heart, a fleshy heart and
take away their stony: that he will put a new spirit and the fear of him in
their hearts, and make us to walk in his precepts. In the Gospel also the
Lord speaking of the fruit of righteousness, testifies, that we be able to do
nothing without him. Ioh. 15.
Again, ye have not chosen me, but I have chosen you and I have appointed you
to bring forth fruit, and that your fruit should remain. 1. Cor. 4.
And Paul says: what have thou* that thou have not received? If thou have
received, why do thou boast, as though thou had not received? And
expressly the same man affirms that repentance is the gift of God. 2. Tim.
2. Gal. 5. Ephesians 5.
As he also reckons faith, and all good works to be the fruits of the
spirit. He testifies also that we are created in Christ Jesus unto good
works, which God has prepared that we should be exercised in them. Ephesians. 1.
And to take away all doubt, he plainly writes, that we are elected before
the foundations of the world were laid, that we should be holy and blameles.
Therefore also the Church prays, both for Infidels and such as resist the
doctrine of the Gospel, that they may be converted unto God: and also for the
faithful, that they may grow in says, and persevere therein. For the things
that he has commanded to be done, would not be requested of God, except it
were his free gift, that they were done.

The 2. error of the Semipelagians.
Other men, albeit they confess that good works and faith also, according to
the growth of it, be of God, yet they will have the beginning of faith to come
of us: as if faith were not given us of God, but only increased of him in us,
by the merit of our belief, or of our good will: and so the good will of a man
should be the cause of Gods grace, and not grace the cause of our will.

This opinion Prosper and Hilarie attribute to the Semipelagians, Epist. ad
Aug. Tom. 7.
or the relics (as they call them) of the Pelagian heresy. And Augustine
himself was sometime in this error, as when he says: I hat we believe, it is
ours; but that we do good, Expos. ad Romans
it is his, who gives to the faithful the holy Ghost. And a little after. To
believe, and to be willing is ours, but to give to the believers, and willers
power to work well by the holy Ghost, that is Gods. But he acknowledged
afterward this error, Lib. 1. cap. 23. and passim lib. de praedest. sanct.
and amended it in his retractions, and elsewhere, where he shows at large,
that not only faith increased, but also begun in us, is Gods gift, the grace
of the caller preventing our will, that we may be willing, according to the
sayings: who first gave to him, and he shall recompense him? because of him,
Romans 1^. Paul 1.
and through him, and in him are all things. To you it is given not only to
believe in him, but also to suffer for his sake. He says simply: to believe,
and not more perfectly to believe. Romans 12.
Like as yt is to the To. God has distributed to every one a measure of faith.
Also to the Ephe. Ye are saved by grace through faith, Ephesians. 2.
and this is not of your selves, it is the gift of God: that is eue^ that which
I said through faith, is not of our selves, but is the gift of God. The same
Apostle says: we are not fit to think any thing, 2. Cor. 3.
as of our selves, then not so much as to believe, because to believe is
nothing else, than to think with assente. To believe what it is,
Also chap. 4. of the 1. Epistle: What have thou that thou have not received?
and if thou have received, why do thou brag, Phil. 2.
as though thou had not received? Expressly also it is said of the will*
that God works in us, both to will and to perform of his good will his
doubtless, and not ours. Therefore it remains firm, that the will is
prevented of God, and that from him it is in ^^^led into us, that we do
believe.

brings faith, at the least the beginning of it, that he may be capable of
grace and the gifts of God. Mayor is particular, seeing God gives some things
even to them that pray not, as the beginning of faith: other things, no
otherwise than by prayer. And thus Augustine answers this doubt, chap. 16. de
bono perseu. and alijs locis.

The 2. error, of the Papists works of preparation.
Thirdly, there be invented of certain men, works I know not what, morally
good, whereby a man before faith and repentance prepares himself to grace,
yea, deserves grace de congruo of conueniencie, as they speak. And that would
they prove by the example of Cornelius, who was endued with the knowledge of
Christ, while the Lord respected his prayers and alms. By these men's opinion
that wicked sentence is maintained, Aug. de praedest. sanct. cap 2. and con. 2.
ep. Pelag. lib. 1. cap. 19.
which Pelagius himself in his episcopal judgment in Palestine condemned,
albeit with an hypocritical heart, to wit, that the grace of God is given
according to our deserts. Beside, when the Apostle says: who has severed
thee? What have thou that thou have not received? by these men's opinion I may
say: my work does sever me, my endeavor, Ephe 2. Heb. 11.
and merit. What? that the scripture teaches that we are all dead in offenses
and sins before faith, and that without this it is impossible that we or our
works should please God. Tell me I pray, what good will had Paul, and not
rather a great wicked will, that breathing forth slaughter, went forward
destroying Christians in the horrible blindness of his mind? By what merits
of his will, by what preparation of works did God convert him from these evils
to faith? In like manner how many enemies of Christ daily are drawn of a
sudden by the secert grace of God to Christ? Lastly the Apostle cries: Tit.
3.
Not through the works of righteousness, which we have done, but according to
his mercy he has saved us. Wherefore away with such preparations, whereby men
are supposed to dispose themselves for grace, that they may be ingrafted into
Christ. De praede. sanct. cap. 6.
Augustine plainly recites this error, reproving the common talk of men,
saying, This or that man therefore deserved to believe, because he was good,
before he believed. As for the example of Cornelius he there answers, that
he was not wholly void of faith. The faith of Cornelius.
For how should he call upon him, in whom he did not believe? How were his
prayers and alms without faith acceptable before God? yet afterward by Peter's
ministry he believed in Christ that now he might know the son of God
incarnate, and might receive the sacrament of regeneration.

The last error about vocation, whereof we speak, The 4. error, making
effectual calling to be in man's power and will.
is the error of certain men that extenuate the effecacie of it, as though the
effect thereof were in the power of a man, that if he will not, God should call
in vain. Yea if a man will not, and goes on to resist grace that is offered
him, it is most certain, that he is not as yet partaker of this calling. Ioh.
6.
For Christ witnesses that this calling is most effectual: Every one that has
heard and learned of the father, comes unto me. De praedest. sanct. cap. 8.
They that hear and learn of God, do come, but they that come not when they
are called, have neither heard nor learned of God.
Which words Augustine considering, says, If every one that has heard of the
father, and has learned, comes, truly every one, that does not come, has
neither heard nor learned. For if he had heard and learned, he would come. For
not any one has heard and learned that comes not: but every one (as says
the truth) who has heard and learned, comes. This grace therefore which of
the divine bountifulnes is secretly given to the hearts of men, is refused of
no hard heart: For therefore it is given that the hardness of the heart might
first of all be taken away, according to the saying: I will take from you a
stony heart, and give you a fleshly. Look upon the same Augustine ad Simpl.
li. 1. quaest. 2. The effect of Gods mercy (says he) cannot be in man's power
that he should in vain show mercy if man will not: Man's will cannot resist
Gods call, nor make his mercy in vain.
because if he please to have mercy on the^ that resist him, he is able so to
call them, as they may most fitly be moved and may understand, and so follow
him. For albeit he calls many, yet he has mercy on them, whom he so calls,
as it is fittest for the^ to be called, that they may follow, neither has he
mercy on any in vain. Therefore they are elected, who are so called, that
they refuse not him that calls them: the rest, are not elected, because they
do not follow, although they be called. The same doctrine of the efficacy of
this calling Luther notably confirms lib. de sermon. arbit. cap. 45.

Some obey their calling because God makes them willing. Aug cant. dua^ epist.
Pelag. cap. 19.
No man surely can believe, but he is willing thereto; for how should a man
believe against his will? But he makes him willing of an vnwiller, who when
he dra^eth us, we come to Christ. Therefore the elect are sundri ways drawn
to be willing, by him who knows inwardly to work in the very hearts of men
not that men should believe against their will (which cannot be, but that of
unwilling persons they might be made willing.

Aug. de praedest. sa^nct. cap. 8. and 9.
But why does he not so draw all? Therefore be unwilling to judge of this
thing, if thou will not err, says Augustine in a place upon Saint John.

CHAP. XV. Other effects of election, i. Justification and glofication: where
also is entreated of the perseverance of the Elect.
The 3. effect of election. Justification.
But of vocation enough. I come to the third principal effect of election, to
wit, Justification. For by the testimony of Paul, Whom he calls, them he
also justifies. And how necessary this benefit is to the elect for the
obtaining of their appointed end of life and heavenly glory, it is manifest to
every one. For seeing we are all under sin, and for sin are guilty of
wrath, and eternal damnation; we cannot see the kingdom of heaven, unless we
be absolved from sins, What it is to be justified.
and accounted just before God, by the free forgiveness of them. And this is
nothing else, than to be justified as the word is here taken. So Paul Romans 4.
defines justification, by the imputation of righteonsnesse: And this he
teaches to consist in the forgiveness of sins, alleging hereunto the
testimony of David, Psalm 32. Blessed are they whose iniquities are forgiven,
and whose sins are covered. Blessed is the man, to whom the Lord imputes not
sin. Justification diversely taken in Scripture.
Otherwise diversely the word Justification is taken in the scriptures. For one
while it signifies the acknowledging and declaring of one as that God is said
to be justified in his sayings: Psalm. 58. Luke 6. verse. 29. and 39.
And Luke shows that the people, when they heard Christ, justified God. And
Christ says, that wisdom is justified of her children. Another while it
signifies to be endued with inherent righteousness, by the infusion of new
qualities, and by good works. As Apoc. 22. it is written: He that hurts, let
him hurt as yet, and he that is just, let him be justified as yet. Thirdly, by
the propriety of the Hebrewe tongue, the judicial signification as I may say
of the word is most usual in the sacred Scriptures, namely, that to be
justified, is to be absolved from guiltines, the word being taken relatiuely,
and not noting any quality. So it is used Proverb 17. verse. 15. Isa. 5.23* And
this signification agrees with our business in hand, seeing the question is
of the justification of a sinful man before God. Certainly therefore are we
justified of him, when we, that are in our selves guilty of hell before the
judgment seat of God, are nevertheless by his grace discharged from all
guiltines, so that the adversary cannot any more condemn: seeing he reputeth
us freely just through redemption in Christ Jesus, by faith in his blood, and
not of works.

To him that is justified, the holy Ghost is given to renew his heart, and to
stir him up to good works
And albeit in the justification of a wicked man, the holy Ghost is withal
freely given, to renew the heart, and to infuse new qualities, whereby we are
stirred up to the study of good works: yet by this newnes, the guiltines of
former sins is not blotted out, neither are we accepted then for our works;
but it is needful, that first we be accepted, that our works being imperfect
and defiled, may be able to please him. But to handle these things more
specially, it is not now material. Furthermore this must be observed, that
the Apostle teaches, Justification proper to the elect.
that this gift of free justification arises of predestination, and he makes
it proper to the elect, after they have bin partakers of a divine vocation. For
he says: Whom he has predestinate, he has called: and whom he has called,
them he has also justified. He says not (as the spirit of error imagines)
that all, reprobates as well as elect, called, and not called, how many soever
are of Adam borne sinners, are justified also in Christ, and received into
grace, being freed from all sin, and the judgment of God. And that no doubt
may remain, explaining himself, Paul adds: What shall we say then to these
things? If God be for us, who can be against us? who spared not his own son,
but gave him for us all. Who shall accuse the elect of God? It is God that
justifies, who shall condemn? to wit, Gods elect. It is Christ who is dead,
yea who is also risen again. We hear of the elect, that justification is
theirs: that Christ for all them was delivered to death. Their opinion also is
withal re^elled, that say, that some even of the number of the reprobates are
justified, but that reconciliation is in them made frustrate. Augustine better
agrees with the meaning of the Apostles saying: Contra Julian. lib. 5. cap. 3.
Those that are not in the number of the predestinate, God brings none of them
to saving and spiritual repentance, whereby a man is reconciled to God in
Christ. The like words are in the same author in Soliloquijs, cap. 28.

The fourth effect of election, glorification.
The fourth effect of predestination of the elect follows, to wit,
glorification. For whom he calls and justifies, them he also glorifieth,
says the Apostle. For he uses verbes of the preter tense for the present
tense, according to the Hebrews manner of speaking. And glorification
signifies the kingdom of the Saints in the heavenly life and withal
comprehends or presupposes the gift of perseverance unto the end, whereof it
is said: He that continues to the end, shall be saved. Matthew. 10.
Like hereto are the promises, Ioh. 6. Whatsoever the father gives me, comes
unto me: and him that comes unto me, I will not cast forth. And straightway:
This is the will of him that sent me; that whatsoever he has given me, thereof
I should lose nothing, but that I should raise it up in the last day. Again,
No man comes unto me, except the father draw him. And I will raise him up at
the last day. The demonstration is plain:

Whosoever by the drawing of the father comes unto Christ, he shall be raised
up by him unto the resurrection of life.

But all come, who are given to Christ, that is, are predestinate in him.

Therefore all they shall be raised up by him unto the resurrection of life,
that is, they shall be glorified.

And this perseverance, which I mentioned, is, Perseverance of the Saints what
it is. Romans 8.35.
not to be overcome with temptations, and (as Paul describes it at large)
neither by oppression, nor anguish, nor any other creature to be separated
from the love of God, but in all things to be more than a conqueror, through
him that has loved us. And this is to be noted in this place concerning that
great benefit of perseverance, both that all the elect have it: and also that
they have it not of themselves, but from another, to wit, from the bountifulnes
of Gods grace. As for the former, the testimonies are plain. Matthew. 24.24.
Romans 8.35. Also 2. Tim. 2. The elect have it. Matthew. 10.
The foundation of God stands sure. Furthermore, when the holy Ghost
testifies that salvation is laid up for such as persevere, and the crown of
life for them that overcome: Apoc. 2. 2. Tim. 4.8.1^. Ioh. 17. Luke 22. Aug. de
Cor. and gra. cap. 8.
they necessarily persevere, that are elected to reign with Christ. Thirdly,
Christ universally prayed for his elect, and was heard, no less than it is
read that he prayed for Peter, that his faith should not fail. Wherein what
did he else pray for, but that he might have in faith a most free, strong,
unconquerable, and perseuering will? Seeing therefore Christ prays for the
elect, that their faith fail not, it shall continue doubtless unto the end,
neither shall the end of this life find it otherwise than abiding: Aug. de
Cor. and gr. cap. 11. and 12 It is Gods gift, proved by three reasons.
as by Peter's example Augustine gathers.

And now that perseverance is Gods gift, both the feeling

Aug. de bono pers. cap. 2.
Secondly, the testimonies hereof are most evident in the writings of the
Prophets and Evangelists. Ierem. 32. says the Lord, I will put my fear in
their hearts, that they may not depart from me. Which thing what is it else,
than, such and so great the fear of me shall be, that I will put in their
hearts, that they shall cleave unto me continually? Hos. 2. I will marry thee
unto my self for ever in righteousness and faith etc. And he has married
all the true believers unto himself. Ioh. 10. I give to my sheep eternal
life, neither shall they perish for ever, and no man shall take them out of my
hand. My father who gave them me, is greater than all. Phil. 1. He that has
begun in you a good work, will accomplish it unto the end.

Thirdly, all the Saints ask perseverance of God in prayer, and in the whole
Lords prayer almost, when the Saints use it, no other thing welneere is
understood to be requested than perseverance, specially when we say, Lead us
not into temptation, but deliver us from evil. And perseverance should be in
vain desired of God, if he gave it not. For it is a mere mockery, to ask
that of God that thou think he does not give, but that it is without his
gift in the power of man. This is Augustines reason, de bono perseu. cap. 2.

Against perseverance, the first objection. 2. Tim. 1. and 4.
many fall away from the faith and make shipwreck thereof. Thus they reason:
All believers are elect, because as Paul says, faith is proper to the elect.
But some believers do fall away. Therefore some elect do fall away. What
believers fall away.
the first proposition is only true of a justifying and saving faith. And that
being settled on the foundation, suffers no man to perish. But many fall away
from an historical and temporary faith, as we call it: while that after they
have embraced the Gospel, they fall away again, and clean to the doctrines
of devils under the pretense of Christian profession. Thus Chrysostom and
Theophylact do expound the testimonies of Paul.



This instance is answered by observing the doubtful signification of the
word, whereof I gave warning at the first: to wit, Elect taken two ways.
that by elect sometime strictly are meant such as be foreordained to eternal
life: sometime generally, whosoever belong by outward calling to the people of
God. For who can deny them to be elect, whom we see profess with us the same
faith and christian conversion? They be plainly called and counted elect in
the judgment of charity, De Cor. and gr. cap 7.
of those that know not what they shall be, says Augustine, and not of him
that knows them to be without perseverance, which leads the elect to a
blessed life. Hereupon John one of the number of the predestinate, who had
sucked this secret out of the Lords breast, pronounces of such: 1. Ioh. 2.
They went out from us, but they were not of us: for if they had been of us,
they had surely continued. What is this I pray you, they were not of us? were
not both created of God? both borne of Adam? were not both called, and become
members of the Church by receiving of the Sacraments? These things are true,
and in respect of all these, they were of us. De bono pers. cap. 3. Why some
fall away.
Yet in respect of another difference they were not of us, because they were
not called according to the purpose of God: they were not in Christ elected
before the creation of the world: they had not obtained an inheritance in him:
Why backsliders are among the godly,
they were not predestinate according to the purpose of him who works all
things. For if they had bin in this state, they had bin of them, and without
doubt had continued with them. But therefore God mixeth in the number of his
Saints some that be not true Saints, nor shall continue, ^ Cor. 10.
least we should be secure. For security is not expedient in the temptation of
this life for the elect: but he that seems to stand let him beware lest he
fall.

Further, unto the assumption of the reason we answer, that even the true
believers and godly, The faithful and elect fall sometime grievously, but they
^^^e again.
and consequently the elect grievously sometime fall from their profession and
Christian life. But partly their faith in their change i^ rather in a deep
sleep, than quenched wholly by such falls: partly it is repaired before they
die, and the sin which they had committed being forgiven, perseverance unto the
end is imputed unto them, as the examples of David and Peter declare. And this
is it that the Psalmist sings: Psalm 37.
The righteous if he fall, shall not be beaten down, because the Lord
vpholdeth his hand.

Apoc. 3. 1. Cor. 10.
Hold that thou have, least another take thy crown. He that seems to stand,
let him take heed least he fall. Romans 11.
Art thou ingrafted into the people of God? fear, for if thou continue not,
thou shall also be cut off. These and such like seem to leave doubtfully the
perseverance of them, to who^ they be spoken. And they be spoken to all, even
to the elect and godly.

Two causes why ye church is to take heed least it fall.
I answer, that such exhortations are usual in the sacred Scriptures, both

CHAP. XVI. Of the effects of Reprobation.
But as the effect of election is not only that grace, whereby we are here
justified, and furthered to live well and continue in goodness, but also whereby
we shall be hereafter glorified: So also the effect of Gods reprobation,
whereby from everlasting he by not electing has reprobated some, Two effects
of reprobation, forsaking or hardening to this life, and damnation in the ^^^e
to come. Lib. 1. ad Moni^^um, cir^a. f^^^m.
is considered in two respects: the first, what cleaves unto the reprobates in
this life: the other, what shall follow and overtake them in the world to come.
And these are, eternal damnation and forsaking, which is also called
hardening, and blinding, where with the just God reuengeth the unjust. Hereupon
says Pulgentius: Destruction is well rendered of God unto evil men, who now
are justly forsaken, and hereafter shall be justly tormented. For in such men
God begins his judgment by desertion or forsaking them, and perfectly
ends it by tormenting them.

But let us hear what the Scriptures witness of them. Matthew. 25. is
manifestly showed, Matthew. 25
that God has not only prepared a kingdom, where the good shall rejoice, but
also eternal fire, where the evil shall be tormented. For he will say to the
good, Come ye blessed of my father possess the kingdom prepared for you from
everlasting: and to the wicked, Go ye cursed into eternal fire, which is
prepared for the devil and his angels. And chap. 10. of Matthew. Chap. 10.
Fear not them that kill the body, but cannot kill the soul: but rather
fear him, who is able to destroy the body and soul in hell. 2. Pet. 2. 2.
Pet. 2.
The Lord knows to deliver the godly out of temptation, and to reserve the
unjust unto the day of judgment to be punished. If therefore the damnation of
hell be the work of God, he has also foreknowne, that is predestined from
everlasting them, Fulgent. lib. 1. and 2. ad Mo^^^.
upon whom he will inflict the same. For his predestination is the preparation
of his works, which in his eternal decree he did foreknow that he would
either in mercy or justice bring to pass.

Apoc. 20. Apoc. 20.
The books were opened and judgment was given of every one according to their
works, and he that was not found written in the book of life, was cast into
the lake of fire. What, that the reprobates are called vessels of wrath and
prepared for destruction? For to be a vessel of wrath, as Augustine
expounds, is, Epist. 10^.
for a man to be appointed to be punished for sins, What a vessel of wrath
is.
who was created for the benefit of nature. And Fulgentius says: Hereunto God
formed the vessels of wrath, whereunto he predestined them, that is, not to
sin, but to destruction for sin. Therefore the destruction of them that
perish, is the work of God reprobating them, and therefore it is the effect of
reprobation.

Thy perdition is of thy self O Israel, but only in me is thy help.

The reward of sin is death: but the gift of God is eternal life through Jesus
Christ our Lord.

Those sayings also are wont to be objected: God made not death. Wisd 1. and.
2. Eze. 18.
Through the envy of the devil death came into the world. Again, I will not
the death of him that dies, etc.

But here with a deaf ear we must not forget, what elsewhere we read,
Eccles. 11. Wisd 16. Deuteronomy. 32.
that death and life, good and evil come of God. Which show of contrariety to
take away, we must understand, that death as well as life may be understood
not two only, but also three manner of ways. For in the first man God created
both the soul and flesh also immortal. But while man sinned, Three deaths, of
the soul, body, and hell.
the soul dyed, and that death of the soul, to wit sin, is the beginning
and cause of another double death, corporal and infernal. The sacred
Scriptures call it the first and second death. Therefore God made not the death
of the soul, because he made not sin: but the devil is the author of it by
suggesting of sin: and by consequence he is the author also of the other
kinds of death, which arise from sin, to wit, in respect of the vehement
stirring up of it, and not that he has power to punish as God has. Augustine
distinctly says: Cont. Julian. lib. 7. cap. 7.
The devil, the deceiuer of man is the cause of death, which God inflicted,
not as the first author, but as the punisher of sin.

Some understand the place of the Prophet Ezechiel of that death of the soul,
as Fulgentius: I will not the death of a sinner.
others refer it to the punishment of sin, using the distinction of the
will of God, hidden and revealed. So Luther de ser. arbit. cap. 109. He will
not the death of a sinner, to wit, by his word, while by the word of salvation
he comes to all, and so he will have all men to be saved. But he wills the
same by his unsearchable will Which will (says he in the same book chap.
107.) is not to be searched into, but with reverence to be a^ored, as the
highest secret of Gods majesty. Again, He will not the death of him that
dies simply, and as it brings destruction, but as it is a punishment: for
the Lord delights not in the perdition of the living, Wis^. 1.13.
as it is written. But he is the punisher of sinners.

Now as touching the matter of forsaking, blinding, Of forsaking, hardening,
and blinding. Romans 9. and 11.
and hardening, I will produce a few testimonies of many. We read in the
sacred Scriptures: He has mercy on whom he will, and whom he will he
hardens. The elect have obtained it, but the rest were hardened, as it is
written: God has given them the spirit of slumber, eyes that they should not
see, and cares that they should not hear. See the prophecy of Esaias 29. verse.
10. and chap. 6. Go and say to the children of Israel: In hearing ye shall
hear, and shall not understand: and seeing ye shall see and shall not
perceive: harden the hearts of this people, and make their heirs heavy, and
smeere over their eyes, least they should see with their eyes, and hear with
their ears, and understand with their hearts, and so be converted, and I
should heal them. Which prophecy S. John alleging, affirms, Ioh. 12.
that the Jews believed not in the Lord, albeit they had seen many signs,
neither that they could believe, because he had blinded their eyes, and
hardened their hearts. To the same unbelieving Jews the Lord said: Ioh 10.
Ye do not believe, for ye are not of my sheep. My sheep hear my voice, and
I know them, and they follow me. Again to his disciples: Matthew. 13.
To you (says he) it is given to know the mysteries of the kingdom of heaven:
but to others it is not given: therefore in parables I speak unto them.

Neither came it to pass without cause, that in so many hundred years before
the coming of Christ, Acts 14.
no light of saving doctrine was revealed to the Gentiles: but as Paul
witnesses, the living God that made heaven and earth, passing over the former
ages, suffered all nations to walk in their own ways, until the coming of
the time of grace he was found of them, that sought him not. And in Israel that
was fulfilled that the Prophet says: Esaias 65.
All the day long I have stretched out my hands unto a rebellious and
gainsaying people.

By these things it is evident, that they that were not ordained to life, are
also rejected from the grace of faith and conversion, and are left in their
own obstinacy: while they either be deprived of the means to hear the
word, or else if they do hear it, are not so called, as that they are become
fit to follow it. We speak now of total blinding (as I may so call it) which
has joined with it final impenitencie: and not of every particular one, which
happens to the predestinate also, whom God now and then before and after
their conversion suffers to fall into sins, yet so, as all things work unto
their salvation.

The use of these things is, that we may understand what great thanks are to
be given to God for his mercy, Aug. ad Simpl. lib. 1. q. 2. The use of all.
who shows in the punishment of some, what he freely bestows upon others.

CHAP. XVII. How God is said to harden and blind.
IT remains now that we consider, how blinding and hardening of the ungodly
is attributed unto God. For unless this be rightly understood, God shall seem
to be accused of injustice, and contrariwise sinners excused, according to the
saying: Why is he yet angry? for who shall resist his will? Some, to avoid
these rocks, do expound, Diverse opinions of Gods hardening a man.
that God hardens by permitting only, and all seem not to take this
permission after one sort: some refer it to the long suffering, and lenity
of God, so that he is said to harden when he does not forthwith chasten
sinners: and to have mercy, when by and by he inuiteth them to repentance by
afflictions. Others say, that permission here is the privation of grace, so
that to harden is all one with, not to soften by grace.

The former exposition is referred of Erasmus to Origene and Hierome. Collat.
de lib. arbit. De ser. arbit. ca. 139. and seq.
But it is confuted by Luther by most strong reasons. For that overmuch
liberty of interpreting, tends to confound all things by a new and unheard
of Grammar: as when God says, I will hard the heart of Pharao, The first
opinion confuted as false.
changeing the persons, a man should take it, Pharao hardens him self by my
lenity. God hardens our heart, that is, we harden our selves, while God
deferreth punishment. So, that God shows mercy, does not signify, to give
grace, to forgive sins, to justify, or to deliver from evils: but contrarily
it signifies to punish, and to chastise. This is nothing else than of mercy
to make wrath, of wrath mercy, by an altogether cross kind of speech. And if
then God be said to harden, when he does good, and forbeareth; and to have
mercy, when he afflicts and chastises, then God shall not be said to have
hardened Pharao, but contrariwise to have had mercy on him. For what omitted
he in afflicting, chastening, and calling Pharao to repentance? These and many
other things, says Luther in that place.

Augustine also reproves Julian the Pelagian of an error for this, Lib. 5.
cap. 3.
that he thought that hardening pertained only unto Gods patience, and not to
his power, as God did not harden, but by showing his patient goodness, when
evils be done. What is it (says he) that we daily say: Lead us not into
tempration? but that we be not delivered over unto our lusts? Do we happily
ask this of God, that his goodness be not patient toward us? What man in his
right wits so means? Because so we should not call for his mercy, but
rather provoke his anger.

The second opinion better, but imperfect.
Their judgment is somewhat fuller, who albeit they also make mention of
permission in this matter, yet they take hardening for the withdrawing and
depriving of grace. And this is it that is read in Augustine: De pr^^l. and
gra^ca. 4. Epist. 1^5. Lib. 1. cap 2.
God is said to harden, to blind, to turn away him whom he will not
soften, inlighten, and call. Neither does God harden, by bestowing malice, but
in not bestowing mercy. Also to Simplician, Gods hardening is, that he is
unwitting to show mercy, so that he gives not any thing to a man to make him
worse, but only gives him nothing to make him better. And by and by; he
bestows not upon some sinners his mercy to justify them, and therefore he
is said to harden some sinners, because he has not mercy on them and not
because he compels them to sin. In this sense Lombard also and Aquinas
speak of induration o^ hardening. Lom. lib. 1. did. 4. c. 4. q. ^. 2. q. 29.
God moves men's hearts to good and evil, but diversely, yet always justly.


Albeit this exposition be tolerable, yet the same Aquinas in his exposition
upon the 9. to the Romans upon the saying, he hardens whom he well: freely
confesses, that something more must be understood herein: and he adds, that
men are moved of God to good or evil by a certain inward motion, but
diuersely. For a man is stirred up to good of God, directly, and of him self,
as of the author of goodness: but unto evil by occasion. Here with may those
things be compared, that Luther has in his book of servile free will cap.
150. and 154. and Brentius upon 1. Sa. 2. of Elies sons writes thus:
Brentius.
To the intent they might be punished worthily according to their deserts, the
Lord by his secret power brought it to pass, that they should not repent at
their fathers admonition, and should perish: to wit, inwardly working what
outwardly he forbids by his word. And this is not to be the author or cause
of maliciousnes, but it is to inflict just punishment for the same. These
things he.

De gra. and lib. ar^. cap. 1.
But especially Augustine at large proves and declares, that God works as
pleases him even in the hearts of wicked men, by rendering unto them according
to their deserts. So God (says he) wrought in the heart of Amasia, 2. King 14.
(with whom doubtless God was justly angry for his Idolatry) that he should
not hear good counsel, but contemning it, should go to war, where with his
whole army he might be destroyed. And in the Psalms it is said of the
Egiptians, what God did unto them. Psalm. 105.
He turned their hearts, that they hated his people, and dealt deceitefully
with his servants.

Of these and such like places of the holy Scripture he afterward concludes,
God works in m^ns hearts, to bow their wills to good or evil.
that God works in the hearts of men to bow their wills, whither soever it
pleases him, either to good of his mercy, or to evil according to their
deserts, in his judgment doubtless, sometime manifest, sometime hidden, but
always just. In like manner against Julian: Lib. 5 cap. 3.
Many things (says he) we could rehearse, whereby it might plainly appear,
that by the secret judgment of God the heart is made perverse, God punishes
sin with sin.
that a man hears not the truth, and thereupon sins for a punishment of
some former sin. And among other things these he mentions: Romans 1. Therefore,
(that is for a recompense of their error) God gave them up into shameful
affections, and into a rebate mind, to do the things that were not convenient,
being full of all unrighteousness, fornication, wickedness, coueteousnes, etc. 2 Thessalonians. 2. Because they received not the love of the truth, that they might be
saved, God sent unto them a strong force of error that they might believe a
lie. Isa. 19. God has cast among them the spirit of error, and they have
seduced Egypt. And chapter. 63. why have thou made us O Lord to err from thy
ways? have thou hardened our hearts, that we should not fear thee? 1. King.
22. By Gods commandment, and in his judgment a lying spirit went forth to
seduce Achab by the false Prophets. He does these things after a wonderful and
unspeakable manner, Augustine.
(says Augustine) who knows how to work his just judgments, not only in
the bodies, but also in the hearts of men: who makes not evil wills, but uses
them, as he will, seeing he cannot will any thing unjustly. Cypr ser. de ^rat.
dom. in 5 petit.
Cyprian also upon the petition. Lead us not into temptation, or as he reads:
Suffer us not to be lead etc. writes, that the adversary can do nothing
against us, unless God before has permitted him, The devil has power against
us in two respects.
and thence is power given him: and that that evil one has power against us
in two respects: either to punishment, when we have sinned, or else to glory,
when we are tried. Which thing in so many words welneere our Augustine
expresses: De sermon. Dom. in monte lib. 2.
Temptations (says he) are made by Satan, not by his authority, but by Gods
sufferance, either to punish men for their sins, or else to prove and exercise
them of Gods mercy.

Therefore by all these proofs a man may after a sort perceive, Gods
hardening is not only permission, but also the operation of his wrath.
that hardening, when in the Scriptures it is attributed to God, signifies not
only permission, but also the operation of Gods wrath: for, (that I may say
nothing of outward things, which make to the induration of reprobates,) the
devil himself, who works mightily in the children of disobedience, is so in
the power of God, that he does nothing but at his commandment. God is the
revenger, and not the author of sin.
And this is diligently to be marked, that, whether by forsaking, or by any
other way that man can show or not show, God, who is most highly good, and
unspeakably just, does exercise such kind of judgments, he is not the author,
but the revenger of crimes, while he punishes one sin with another. For we
seek for the merit of mercy (says Augustine) and we find it not, Ad Sixtum.
Epist. 105.
because there is none; least grace should be made of none effect, if it be
not given freely, but rendered for deserts. But we seek for the merit of
hardening, and we find it. For, for good cause the whole lump of sin is
condemned from the first beginning of it: Romans 5.
and as age increases, so wickedness thereupon grows through a man's own
will, and thereupon also damnation. Therefore the Scriptures rehearse
delivering up into evil lusts, and efficacy of error and such like, as the
scourges of Gods wrath, and the just recompense of reward.

But God works (thou say) hardness of heart. But hardness of heart is sin:
for what is it else, than to resist the commandment of God as Stephen cast the
Jews in the teeth? Therefore God works sin.

Contra Iul. pel. lib. 5. cap. 3.
in the Minor there is a fallacy of the accident. For (as Aug. teaches,) the
hardness of heart, as many other things, may be considered three manner of
ways: to wit, as it is sin: as it is the punishment of former sins: Hardness
of heart taken three ways.
and as it is the cause of sins following, namely, when through the error of
a blind heart some evil is committed. And these diverse respects concur
accidentally in one and the same work. Therefore as far forth as hardness
(whereof we speak) is a sin, it is the work of man and the devil: and it is
Gods work no otherwise than as it is apunishment. For he is the author of
every punishment of sin whatsoever it be: because every punishment of sin, as
it is such, is good, because it is just. But God is not the author of sin,
because it is evil. And God is exceeding good and just, and cannot be the
author of an evil and unjust thing, as it is written: Thou art a God that have
no pleasure in wickedness. Psalm. 5. Lib. 5. Stromat.
And Clemens Alexandrinus cites out of the Poet: Jupiter who reigns over the
Gods, and beholds all things, is not the cause of evils to mortal men.

Hereupon depends an answer to an other objection also: That if God hardens
men, they that sin, They that be hardened, have no defense for it, and that for
two causes.
seem to be made excusable. For who may resist his will? that God hardened
him, Exodus 8. Wisd. 2.
but also that he hardened his own heart. And the wiseman witnesses, that the
wicked err, because their own malice blinds them. For through malice they
shut their own eyes, least they should see, and stop their ears, least they
should hear, like the deaf adder that stops her ear, Psalm. 58.
least she should hear the voice of the charmer.

CHAP. XVIII. Whether God has predestined the infidelity of the reprobates,
and sins in general.
ANd these things of the effects of reprobation. But here further the question
was wont to be asked, i. Whethetr reprobates, as they are said to be
predestined to suffer damnation for sins, so also be predestined to sins
themselves, infidelity and others, whereby they draw damnation upon
themselves, or no? And generally, whether God predestined any man to sin?
Some men surely fear not to avouch it, as not repugnant unto the truth,
Anselme. lib. de Concord. gra. and lib. ^ib.
nor inconvenient. Of which opinion is Anselme, Bishop of Canterbury, who was
famous in the year 1060. for he boldly affirms, that there is a
predestination not only of good things, but may be said also to be of evil
things after a sort because evil things are not done, unless God do permit
them. Whereupon (says he) after this sort it is not inconvenient, that God
has predestined evil things, while he corrects not evil men, and their
evil works: but he is said more specially to foreknowe and predestinate good
things, because he makes them both to be, and to be good: but in evil things
he causes only that they essentially be, and not that they be evil.

Fulgent. lib. 1. ad Mon.
Contrariwise, Fulgentius in his first book to Monimus, (where plentifully, and
of purpose he handles this question) defines, Sins are foreknowne, but not
predestinate to be done, yet punishment for ^^^e, is predestinate of God.
that the good and righteous God foreknew surely the sins of men, (because
nothing that was to come, could be hid from him) yet that he predestined no
man to sin. This opinion he confirms by the definition of predestination,
which in his judgment is nothing else, than the preparation of the works of
God: or the merciful and just disposition of a divine work that should come
to pass. Seeing therefore that iniquity pertains not to the work of God,
which he knows how to punish, and not to commit; it will follow of the
definition already brought, that a man is not predestined of God to commit
sins. He brings also his reason: That sinners should be unjustly punished
of God, if by his will they should sin, as being predestined of him to
sin. Aug. de pred. san^^. cap. 10. How pred^stination and foreknowledge
differ.
Further, he cites Augustine and Prosper to be of the same mind. For
Augustine in that notable work, that he wrote of the predestination of
Saints, chap. 10. distinguishing between Gods foreknowledge and
Predestination, says: Predestination cannot be without foreknowledge: but
foreknowledge may be without predestination. How predestination and
foreknowledge differ.
For by predestination God foreknew the things that he would do: but he was
able to foreknow even the things that he does not, as all sins. For albeit
some things are so sins, that they be also punishments of sins: yet here
sin belongs not to God, but judgment. The same man unto the Articles
falsely laid to his charge, Art. 7.8 9. and quibusdam. seq.
(when the Pelagians spitefully traduced the doctrine of predestination) as
though he would make God the author of sins: refuting that detestable and
abominable blasphemy at large, thus writes: The Lords predestination is never
without goodness, never without justice for all the ways of the Lord are mercy
and truth. And the holy deity liked not to prepare the adulteries of
matrones, and the deflouring of maidens, but to condemn them, nor to appoint
them, but to punish them. And in the same place: Gods predestination has not
stirred up, moved, or caused the falls of them that perish, nor the malice of
wicked men, nor the iniquities of sinners, but he has altogether predestined
his judgment, whereby he will render to every one as he has done, be it good
or evil: which judgment should not be, if men should sin by the will of
God. Also, There is no predestination of God for the transgression of the law,
and the committing of any kind of sin. It is sufficient to know that the
standing of things be of him, and that the ruins of things be not of him:
neither does it follow, (as they that object such things, suppose) that God
has taken away repentance from them, to whom he has not given it: and that he
has thrown them down, whom he has not lifted up: seeing it is one thing to
make guilty an innocent person, (which is far from God) another thing not to
have pardoned a wicked man, which belongs to the desert of a sinner. Again,
Aug. Hypognost. 6.
The rule of this matter in question must surely be maintained, (which is
clear by divine testimonies) that sinners in their own evils are foreknowne
only: Ad 14. object. Gallorum. Why infidelity comes not of predestination.
but punishment is predestined for them. Hereunto Prosper a learned and godly
man consents: The infidelity of such as believe not the Gospel is not bred
of Gods predestination. For God is the author of good things, and not of evil.
And in his answer to the 15. objection: God only foreknew, and did not also
predestinate those things, which should not have from him the cause of their
working. To the same purpose did the Council of Araus. determine in their last
canon.

And as for Anselme he disagreeth not so much in sense, as in words from the
things, which these fathers write and judge: seeing he used the word
predestination in a more general signification, for preordination: making it
all one to predestinate, and to decree that a thing should be. And now, there
is nothing at all done, Mark this.
that the Lord is unwilling with all: yet so that we know, that good things are
done by his assistance and working, evil things are permitted, he giving them
over, and ordaining and directing them to such good ends, as it pleases him.
And this is it that Fulgentius also writes, that God surely is not the author
of evil thoughts, Lib. 1. ad Mon.
yet that he is the ordainer of evil wills, and that he does not cease to work
some good of the evil work of every evil man.



Thom. in 9. ad Romans lect. 1.
but they differ, that election imports the preparation of faith and good
works, whereby a man comes to glory: How election and reprobation agree and
differ.
but reprobation does not import a preparation of sins, whereby a man comes
to punishment. Wherefore this only follows, seeing the elect are
predestined to faith and repentance, that they may be holy and blameles
before God, that the reprobates are not predestined to the same grace.



fair forth pertains to Gods predestination, because it is not far from
the working of it. For those things that be so sins, that withal they be
also punishments of sins, in them not sin, but Gods judgment pertains to
Gods predestination, as I have showed out of Augustine.

CHAP. XIX. That predestination is steadfast and immovable.
