THe fourth part of this whole treatise, The necessity of predestination is
immovable.
according to the division set down in the beginning, is of the
unchangeableness of predestination. Where we must consider, that election and
reprobation, namely, the decree of God concerning the either saving of men in
mercy, or the punishing of them in justice, cannot be changed, so that of the
number of the elect any one perishes: or contrarily any of the reprobates be
saved: but as well the one be infallibly saved, as the other be infallibly
damned.

This opinion may be confirmed by many testimonies of the sacred Scriptures.
Ioh. 6. the Lord says: The elect cannot perish, not the reprobates be sau^d;
because Gods decree is unchangeable toward both, proved by many places.
every one that the father gives me, shall come unto me, and all that comes
unto me, I will not cast forth. And chap. 10. My sheep hear my voice, and I
know them, and I give unto them eternal life, neither shall they perish for
ever, and no man shall pluck them out of my hand. My father who gave them me,
is greater then all, neither can any man pull them out of my fathers hand. 1.
Ioh. 2. They went of from us, but they were not of us. For if they had been,
they had surely continued with us. 2. Tim. 2. The foundation of God stands
sure, having this seal: God knows who are his. As also Apoc. 7. there is
mention made of the seal of the living God, and of the certain number of them
that are sealed in their foreheads: to wit, of the servants of God, whom his
grace preserves harmeles, and untouched among the midst of the storms of
calamities upon sea and land. So afterward in the same book, chapter 13. and
17. power is given to the Dragon and beast to seduce the whole earth: yet the
elect are excepted, whose names are written in the book of life, from the
foundation of the world. Therefore the elect cannot be deceived, at the least
finally. As also Mat. 24. False Christ's and Prophets shall arise, and shall
work great signs and miracles, to seduce, if it might be, the very elect.
What means that saying of Paul, Romans 8. We know that all things work for
good to them that love God, that is, that are called of purpose. In Soliloq. c.
28.
All things he says, even evil things, yea sins themselves, as Augustine
witnesses. And most plainly the Apostle confirming the same, adds: For whom
he foreknew, them he predestined also to be made conformable to the image
of his son. Whom he predestined, them he has justified, and glorified
also. What shall we say then to these things? If God be for us, who can be
against us? even he who spared not his own son, but gave him for us all, how
shall he not with him give us all things also? Who shall accuse the elect of
God? who shall condemn? who shall separate us from the love of Christ? Neither
death nor life, etc. And in the 9. chapter of the same Epistle: It cannot be
that the word of God should fail. On whom he will he has mercy, and whom he
will he hardens. Who shall resist his will? And chap. 11. God has not cast
away his people whom he foreknew: which thing he there shows by the example
of Elias times, and of the Jews of his time: among whom albeit far
degenerate, and enemies to the Gospel, yet God reserved to himself a remnant
according to the election of grace. And he annexeth a saying worthy to be
remembered: Israel obtained not that he sought for: but the elect attained it,
and the rest were hardened. And a little after: Therefore as touching the
Gospel they are enemies for your sakes, but in respect of election they are
loved for their fathers sakes. For the gifts and calling of God are without
repentance: that is, surely fixed without changing, as Augustine expounds,
who also warns us to understand this saying, not of that calling whereof it
is said: Many are called, etc. but of that, whereby the elect are called.

Predestination is unchangeable. The 1. reason.
the propounded sentence leans on most effectual reasons. For what things
God from eternity foreknoweth, they must needs certainly and infallibly come
to pass, as they be foreknowne of him. For foreknowledge is the knowledge of a
thing that shall be. And that which shall come to pass, must needs be done.
And it is confessed already, that God foreknoweth who shall be saved, and who
shall be damned. Ergo, etc.

Further, the decrees of God are unchangeable. The 2. reason.
But election and reprobation (as it is said) be the decrees of God, of saving
whom he will in mercy, and condemning whom he will in justice. Therefore it
cannot be, but both election and also reprobation is firm and immutable, so
that neither the elect can perish, nor the reprobates be saved. For of the
immutabilitie of Gods secrets it is said: 1. Sam. 15.2^. Numb. 23.23.
The strength of Israel will not lie, nor repent. For he is not a man, that he
should repent. Also, God is not as a man, that he should lie, nor as the son
of man, that he should be changed. He has said, and shall he not do? he has
spoken, and shall he not accomplish? And in Esaias: The Lord of hosts has
decreed, and who can break it? Esaias 14.^4 27.
The hand of the Lord is stretched out, and who shall turn it away? And, My
counsel shall stand, and I will do all my will. Cap. 46.10.


But in that God now and then denounces and threatens some things, which yet
afterward he does not, A doubt.
by the example of the Ninevites, and of Ezechias: we must understand, that
therein the Scripture speaks according to the nature of second causes, or else
with the condition secretly understood of repentance, and prayer, as Iere. 18.
such like sayings are expounded: and not in respect of Gods simple and absolute
decree. In which sense it is well said of Gregory: The Lord knows how to
change his saying, but never knows how to change his counsel. And of
Augustine: God knows how to change his saying, if we would change our life.

Thirdly, The 3. reason.
examples manifestly prove the infalliblenes of predestination. Pharao, Sehon,
and the kings of the Canaanites with their subjects were appointed to
destruction. What was done? their hearts were hardened, that they might fight
against Israel and perish for their sins. And it is expressly written: Exodus
4. Deuteronomy 2. ^s. 11. 1. Sam. 2. 2. King. 12.
It came of the Lord to harden their hearts to fight with Israel, that he might
destroy them. So Elies sons hearkened not to the voice of their father,
because the Lord had appointed to destroy them. Roboam heard not the people,
because it pleased the Lord by that means to rent the kingdom, 2. Sam. 7.
and to pull it away from Salomons race. Absalom and every man of Israel
despised the good counsel of Achitophel, and heard Husai: because the Lord
minded to destroy Achitophels counsel, to punish Absalom. Likewise nothing of
the word of the Lord against Ahabs house fell to the ground, 2. King. 10.
but the Lord accomplished as he had spoken by his servant Elias.

Genesis 37.39.41.50.
On the other side, how wonderfully was Joseph, when his brethren went about to
kill him, sent into Egypt, and advanced to the government of the kingdom, that
the counsel of the Lord might be fulfilled? What was of less hope in the eyes
of men, than David's reign? Yet he rose to the dignity of the kingdom out of
all his persecutions, and governed the people by the knowledge of his hands;
that the Lords counsel might stand, when all the devises and counsels of the
people were scattered. These things are plain and clear.

Acts 2. and 4.
But that is more notable that we read of Christ himself. Herod and Pontius
Pilate with the Gentiles and people of Israel were gathered together against
him, to do whatsoever the hand and counsel of the Lord had before determined
to be done. Chap. 13.
And when by the determinate counsel and providence of God they had taken him,
being yielded unto them, with wicked hands they crucified and slew him, and
fulfilled all things that the Prophets had written of him, albeit all the while
they went about another thing. What shall we say of the Apostle, and teacher of
the Gentiles? 1. Tim. 1.
who first was a blasphemer, and a persecutor, and an oppressor, how mightily
and miraculously was he called to the Gospel, Gal. 1.
when it pleased God, who had separated him from his mothers womb? R^^. 9 and
10. Esa. ^^.
And to use not so much examples of persons, as of nations: the Gentiles, who
sought not after righteousness, attained unto it, and the Lord was found of
them who sought him not, and made manifest to them that did not enquire after
him: But Israel did gainsay, Romans 11
and seeking his own righteousness, was not subject to the righteousness of
God. For the elect obtained it, the rest were hardened, as it is written: He
gave them the spirit of slumber, eyes that they should not see, and ears that
they should not hear.

Fourthly, The 4. reason.
the same doctrine of the unchangeableness of Gods predestination, is in sundry
places mentioned of Augustine, as cap. 7. de correp. and great. If any of the
elect perishes, God is deceived, and is overcome by man's sin: but none of
them perishes, because God is not deceived, nor overcome by any thing. But he
speaks of such as be elected to reign with Christ, and not in that sense as
Judas was elected to the work whereto he served. And in his answer to the
Articles falsely laid to his charge: The predestination of God, albeit it be
uncertain with us, yet with him, who made the things that shall come to pass,
it abides unchangeable: neither does he darken the things that he
inlighteneth, nor destroys what he does build nor plucketh up what he has
planted. For the gifts and calling of God are without repentance, and the
foundation of God stands sure, having this seal: The Lord knows who are
his. And most notably, in Soliloq. cap. 28. All of us are as a menstruous
cloth, coming out of a corrupt and filthy lump: but thou that only art
clean, do purify them in whom it pleases thee to dwell, whom without
their merits thou have predestined before the world, and chosen for thy self
to be a holy temple, whose names and number thou know, and who cannot
perish, to whom all things work for the best, The Lord knows from
everlasting the names and number of elect and reprobates.
yea even their very sins. For when they fall, they shall not fall down,
because thou put under thine hand, keeping all their bones, that none of
them be broken. But yet the death of sinners is most miserable, (those I
mean) whom (before thou made heaven and earth) according to the great depth
of thy judgments, being secret, yet always just, thou did foreknow for
eternal death, whose names and merits are numbered with thee, who have numbered
the sand of the sea, and fadomed the bottom of the depth: All things work for
the worst to reprobates, yea, even their prayers.
whom thou have left in their uncleanness, to whom all things work for evil,
yea their very prayer is turned into sin, that if they should ascend into
heaven, and make their neast among the stars, they might as dunge perish, and
not in the end.

Likewise the Author of the calling of the Gentiles, lib. 2. cap. 10. says: All
the sons of adoption not only are elect at the time of their calling here in
this life, but also before the world was made: in which election, what man
soever was not in Christ foreknowne, shall by no means be made partaker of
him. And by and by. The foreknowledge of God loses nothing of the fulness of
the members of Christ's body, and the whole sum forknowne, and in Christ
before the world forechosen, cannot be by any hurt diminished. No man can
doubt that this is Luthers very doctrine, Cap. 148.
that reads his book of servile will, where he often mentions, that the
elect are builded up by the word, Cap. 40.
and made better, and that the reprobates are offended, and become worse: that
they amend their life by the holy Ghost, and that the other perish without
amendment: Cap. 59.
and if the elect be in an error, yet that before their death they do come
into the right way: Cap. 152.
that this is necessary, and that they cannot finally be offended. And
praefat. ad Romans he writes, that the decree is stable, and the necessity
immovable of Gods predestination: and he says, that this necessity is very
necessary for our salvation and consolation: because we be so weak in our
selves, that if salvation stood in us, very few or none should be saved: for
the devil would conquer all. But now seeing this sure and most certain decree
of God cannot be changed, nor abolished, by any creature, we have hope that
at length we shall overcome sin, To say that the elect may be increased or
diminished, is Pelagi^nisme. Epist his ad ^^^st.
albeit now it rage in our flesh. These things in that place says Luther. But
as it is a point of sound doctrine, to hold, that ye predestinate number of the
chosen can neither be increased nor diminished: so not to rest therein is
Pelagianisme, as Prosper testifies. Which Hilarie also confirms, reporting
that the remnants of the Pelagians took it hardly, that such as were of sound
faith, preached that men were so predestined both to the one and the other
state, that none could go from one to the other.

By these things then it is manifest, The elect cannot perish.
that such as be predestinate Their number is certain.
and numbereth the sand de Correp. and gra. cap. 13. The number of them that be
predestined to the kingdom of God is so certain, that nothing may be added
to them, nor taken from them. Again ad Laur. cap. 29. The number of the
blessed, whether it be that which is, or that which shall be, is in the eye of
that workman, that calls things that be not, as though they were, and
disposes all things in number, weight and measure. And whereas some think
that the number of

CHAP. XX. Answers to such objections, as are wont to be made against the
vnchaungeablenes of Predestination.
But some object unto us in this place, The sum of the objections of both
kinds.
that a window is opened to impiety, by making as it were sides, whereto
neither must anything be added, nor any thing detracted: and fatal necessity
is brought in: the free will of man denied: the ministry of the word and
prayers taken away: sinners are excused; and (which God forbid) God is accused
as the author of sin: and men are provoked to despair, and such other like,
whereby subtle detractors openly vaunt themselves, as the Pelagians did in old
time, by the report of Prosper and Hilarie. Further beside these consequences
of man's reason naughtily wrested, there be some places of Scripture objected,
but in vain as we shall see.

therefore they cavil, that a window is opened to heinous offenses, because
men thus think: It must needs be done that God has predestined: Therefore
whether I do well or ill, if I be predestined to Gods kingdom, I shall be
saved: if I be not, I shall be damned.

Romans 3.
whose judgment is just, says Paul. But that must not be imputed to the
doctrine, but to the abuse of it. For So Luther represseth those wicked
speeches, on the 26, of Genesis, and elsewhere. For it is all one, as if a man
said: what God has appointed must be; therefore all care of our souls, and
all our labor is uncertain, and to no purpose.

Predestination not only appoints the end, but the means to the end. Romans
8. Ephesians. 1.
whom he has predestined, them also he has called, justified, and
glorified. Also, He has chosen us in him that we should be holy, and
without blame before him. Wherefore, it is a platting of a contradiction that
he who is predestined, can will and do evil finally. Yea rather, as Augstine
writes, they that be of the number of the chosen and predestinate, albeit they
lead a bad life for a time, Contra. I^l lib. 6. cap 3.
yet through the goodness of God they are brought to repentance, and are not
taken out of this life in their sins: for predestination is the preparation of
Gods benefits whereby as many as be delivered, are most certainly delivered.
Therefore that vain and idle reason (as it is called) does not trouble us,
which if we should obey, it is to no purpose whatsoever we do in our life.

But let the objection be turned upon them, that so bring in predestination,
that they separate the means from the end. In old time such was the heresy of
the Predestinati, who, The heresy o^ the predestinati.
(as Sigebert witnesses in Chron) sprung up under Honorius the Emperor, and
Pope Zosimus, were so called of the doctrine, they held: because naughtily
understanding predestination and grace, they avouched, that neither the study
of good works profited them that lived well, if they were predestined of God
to death, nor that the ungodly were hurt by wicked living, if they were
predestined of God to life. By which assertion they withdrew good men from
good things, and stirred up evil men to wickedness.

Another objection was of destiny, The second objection of fate or desteny.
that it was brought in if all things depend on the stable and immutable decree
of Predestination; as for example, who shall receive the word, who not: who
shall believe, who shall not, and thereby who shall be saved, who damned.

I answer. We preach not destiny but the depth of Gods grace, whereby the
difference is made of the elect from the lump of perdition, the rest in the
same lump being forsaken by the just judgment of God. And whereas the order
of Gods grace, and his judgment is immovable, that makes nothing for fate or
destiny: unless peradventure we should take fatum to be derived of fando,
which is of speaking. For we cannot deny, that God once spoke, that is,
immovably and unchangeably decreed, what things he would do, as he knew
unchangeably all things that should be, in which respect (as Augustine
writteth) we may say, De C^uit. Dei lib. 5. cap. 9. The Mathematical fate.
fatum has his name of fando. But this name was wont to be understood in
another matter. For in the common use of speaking, by fate men understand the
force of the position of the stars and planets, as it falls out, when a man
is borne, or conceived, or newly formed, Natural and poetical fate.
and this is the Mathematical fate. Also those things are of many writers
called fatal, which happen beside the will of God, and men, by the necessity
of a certain order, as that verse shows: What once prepared is to be,
Surmounteth Jove his high degree.


And in Homer (the prince of Poets) Jupiter lamenteth, that he could not
deliver from death his dear son Sarpedon, whom fate compelled to die:
Likewise Neptune mourns because he could not hinder the return of Ulysses
into his country, that he might revenge Cyclops his son; for the sates had
decreed that Ulysses should return into Ithaca. And in Ovid the same Jupiter
says: Think thou alone (O daughter dear) from thee
to put away unconquerable fate?
And afterward: The fates that stable be, and do abide for aye,
fear neither planets thunderbolts nor any great decay.


The stoicall sat. De Ciuit. Dei. lib. 5. cap. 5.
Also this opinion is commonly attributed to the Stoicke philosophers, albeit
Augustine does mitigate their opinion) because they said that the knitting
together of things by fate does depend upon Jupiter, whom they supposed to be
the sovereign God. For in Aulus Gellius li. 6. cap. 2. there is Chrysippus his
definition of fate or destiny: What fate is.
that it is the natural and unchangeable conjunction and order of all things
from everlasting. Cicero de fato.
As Cicero also notes the opinion of such me^ as say, that all things are
brought to pass by a natural binding and coupling together without
interruption. These dotings not only the Church, but also sounder phylosophie
rejects and condemns: both because they take from God his liberty and
omnipotency, and also because they abolish the order and manner of working
ordained by Gods wisdom in second causes. For some second causes be ordained
of God to certain and determined effects, other be not, but of their own
nature are indifferent to bring forth this or that effect.

Fate for Gods providence Lib. epist. 18.
Other men do term the connection and order of all causes hanging on the will of
God by the name of fate, which we use to call Gods providence. So Anneus
Seneca: (The fates lead him that is willing, and draw him that is unwilling,)
most evidently called that fate, which in the same place he had called the
will of the highest father. And Apuleius says, In dogma^e Platonis.
that fate is a divine Law, whereby the inevitable purposes and entreprises of
God are brought to pass. De cause lib 4.
After the same manner Boethius spoke many things of fate. For he will have a
fatal order to depend upon the simplicity of the divine providence: How Gods
providence and fate do dister by Boethius.
so that providence is a divine way disposing all things, but fate is a
disposition inherent in moveable things, that is, the execution of that
eternal providence in God. And seeing fate proceeds from the beginning of an
immovable providence, he writes, that hence it comes to pass, that it also
is immutable.

With such men as these be, if we credit Augustine, De Ciuit. lib. 5. cap. 7.
we must not greatly contend about the word, seeing they attribute the very
order of causes, whereby every thing is done that comes to pass, to the will
and power of the Soueragne God, whom most truly we believe, both that he
knows all things before they be done, and also leaves nothing undisposed:
and all powers are from him, Evil wills are not from God.
albeit not all wills proceed from him. For evil wills are not from him, because
they are against nature, which comes from him. Christians must not have the
word fate or destiny in their mouths.
Yet because we must speak of divine things according to the rule of piety,
it is beter to abstain from the word fate or destiny, as Augustine in the
same place notably advises us chapter 1. Humane kingdoms are altogether
appointed by the divine providence, which if a man therefore attribute to fate,
because he understands thereby the very will and power of God, let him hold
his judgment still but let him reform his tongue.

But it is an ungodly and sacrilegious thing, Mark this well.
that some endeavor to find fault with yt order of causes which are coupled
together, which order is certain with God foreknowing and disposing it: such
men must at length needs fall to this point, that they will both deny Gods
foreknoweldge, and also all prophecy, that is clearer then the light, (as
Cicero did, de diuinat. 2. For if all future things be foreknowne, that they
shall in that order come to pass, as they are foreknowne that they shall be.
And if they shall come to pass by this order, the order of things is certain
with God foreknowing them. And if the order of things be certain, the order of
causes also is certain. For not any thing can be done, before which there went
not some efficient cause.

Of freewill.
But in this order of causes that are joined one with another, is there any
liberty of our free will? Does the chain of predestination constrain the
very motions also of men's minds?

Liberty threefold.
I answer, there is a threefold liberty according to the common distinction:
liberty from coaction, from sin, and Of these three, liberty from coaction
or compulsion is the property of the will, that cannot be lost, as Bernard
says, that is, that whatsoever it wills, whether it be that which is good,
by the grace of God: or that which is evil of it one nature, it always
wills it freely, that is, with a voluntary motion, and not by compulsion. We
do surely many things against our will, and compelled: yet the will it self
can desire or choose nothing unwillingly and forced: because if it should will by
constraint, it should unwillingly will, which implies a contradiction. Euchir.
cap 30.
Otherwise as it is in Augustine, man naughtily using freewill, lost both
himself and it, and hereby, he that is the servant of sin, is at liberty
to sin, but to do right cously he is not free, unless being delivered from
sin, he begin to be the servant of righteousness. Moreover, if free be mea^t
that, which is subject to none, nor depends upon any other thing, Liberty
from subjection the will has not.
or is limited, in that sense we must not doubt, that there can be no liberty
of man's will, but that all things are done of necessity, as God has
foreknowne, and works by his infallible counsel, Cap. 16^.
and power, as Luther often mentions in many places in his book of servile
will. For as other things, so the elections of the wills of angels and men, are
subject to Gods providence, Acts 17.
in whom we live, be, and are moved, and who inclyneth the heart of a king,
whither souer he will. ^
Gregory Nyssene in his book that he wrote of man, says: providence is of
such things as be not in us, And Damascene lib. 2. orth. fid. writes,
Damascene.
that God foreknoweth only the things that are in us, and does not foreordaine
them.

But these are dangerous speeches, and ought not or cannot be admitted but
very warily; as Thomas advises us: Tho. cont. gent. lib. 2. cap. 90. Aug. de
Ciuit. Dei. lib 5 cap ^.
And Augustine much better: All wills are subject to the will of God, even all
wills, because they have no power but what he grants them.

But thou will say, What is determined of God, necessarily so comes to pass,
as it is determined. But all actions and motions of the minds of men are
determined of God. Therefore necessarily they so come to pass: and by the
force of the consequent, there is in them no liberty of our will.
necessity of coaction, Necessities twofold.
and immutability. That moves violently through an external beginning only*
but this imposes surely a necessity upon the second causes, that they effect
this or that thing, and thereby also upon the effects, that they so come to
pass: Things in respect of God necessarily come to pass, but in respect of
second causes most things are contingent, and casual.
yet it takes not away the natures of things, but rather confirms them.
Therefore albeit in respect of God, who foreseeth all things infallibly, and
vnchaungeably foreordayneth them, necessarily, that is, infallibly and
vnchaungeably all things come to pass: yet in respect of the second causes,
(them I mean, that are not in their own nature limited to certain effects,
as be all the wills of Angels and men,) many things are done contingently, and
by choice. And it is to be marked, that, seeing effects have their names of
their next causes, such effects are rightly called contingent: even as they,
that arise of second causes necessarily working, (as the motions of heaven, the
heat of fire,) are, and are called necessary: albeit notwithstanding in
respect of God, altering the order of nature, as often as pleases him,
contingencie or chance has also place in such. Examples confirm this
answer. Examples hereof.
As for example Christ must suffer and be slain, yet he suffered and dyed
willingly, as it is written: I lay down my life, Luke 24.
that I may take it again. No man takes it from me. John 1^.
I have power to lay it down, and I have power to take it again. Also, All
things must be fulfilled, which were written of him: yet what was more
casual, considering the natures of second causes, than that the soldiers cast
lots for Christ's coat? that they brake not a bone of him? and such like. In
the Angels also and perfect spirits in heaven, so mighty is the kingdom of
grace, that necessarily they do not sin: shall we therefore deprive them of
the liberty of their will? What shall we say of God himself, who is
unchangeably good, who must needs always live, and foreknow all things? Yet
God forbid that we should put the life and foreknowledge of God under
necessity: as neither is his power diminished, when he is said, that he cannot
dye, De Ciuit. Dei. lib. 5. cap. 10.
or be deceived. Which example Augustine also uses: where he answers in
like manner the foresaid question by that distinction of a twofold necessity.
And chapter 9. he writes, that it does not follow, that if there be with God
a certain order of causes, therefore nothing is in our will seeing in the very
order of causes even our wills are accounted. Anselme writes at large of this
matter, concerning the agreement of Gods foreknowledge and predestination with
freewill.

CHAP. XXI. Answers to the other objections.
Whether the ministry of the word be overthrown by predestination.
But they say, that by the doctrine of Gods unchangeable predestination the
ministry of the word is overthrown, and clean taken away: to wit, all
instructions, exhortations, reproves, consolations, and lastly all doctrine
both publicly and privately. For what things are unchangeably predestined of
God to be done, in vain are means used in them: And such is the salvation of
men: Therefore such means are used in vain.

means, which even he himself pleases to use for the ends appointed of him,
and has also commanded us to use them. And such are those means that are
mentioned in the objection. For it is written: 1. Tim. 4.
Give heed to exhortation and reading: practice these, continue therein: take
heed to thy self and to doctrine. For if thou do this, thou shall save thy
self, and those that hear thee. Also, Be instant in the word, in season, 2.
Tim. 4.
out of season, rebuke, reprove, exhort with all truth and doctrine, watch in
all things. And the Lord himself says: Matthew. 18.
If thy brother sin against thee, go and tell him his fault, etc. if he
hear thee, thou have won thy brother. Iam. 5.19. Iud. verse. 21.
And every where we are commanded to stir up the slothful, to reprove
those that sin that they may repent.

Further it is certain, Predestination comprehends both the end and the
means to the end.
that predestination belongs not only to the end, namely the salvation of
such as be predestinate, but to the means also leading to that end: the word
of God and experience bearing witness, that it is given to very few to receive
the doctrine of salvation by the Lord himself or by Angels, Aug. de bono pers.
cap. 19.
without the preaching ministry of man: and that it is given to many to
believe in God by men. For God observes this order commonly toward his elect,
that whom he has severed from damnation by the bountifulnes of his grace, for
them he procures his Gospel to be heard, and when they hear, moves them to
believe, Aug. de Cor. and great. cap. 7.
and to continue unto the end in says, which works by love, and to repent
upon admonition, if at any time they go astray. Yea and some also he brings
back into the way which they had forsaken, without the reproof of men.

But in the children of perdition it comes to pass that is written, that
they hearing hear not, that is, De bono perse^, cap. 14.
hearing by the sense of the body, they hear not with the assent of the heart.
De Correp. and great. cap. 15.
In the mean while, seeing we know not who belong to the number of the
predestinate, who not, we must be so affected with love, that we wish al to be
saved, warning and reproving all and every one as occasion is offered, with
meekenes, waiting if at one time or other God will give them to acknowledge 2.
Tim. 2.
the truth, and escaping out of the snare of the devil, (of whom they are
held captives) to receive a sounder mind. Therefore let us do our duty,
applying brotherly correction to all men, that they perish not, or destroy
others: but it belongs to God to make the same profitable unto them, We must
do our duty, in ^^rouing o^^^ (...) we do not profit them, and why (...)
who^ he himself has foreknowne, and predestined to be conformable to the
image of his son. And albeit he make it not profitable to all, (for many are
the children of perdition) yet it is sufficient for us, that we do deliver our
own souls, while they perish not through our fault. As the Lord says to
Ezechiel: Cap. 3.
When I shall say to the wicked, thou shall surely die: and thou have not
warned him, nor spoken to him to return from his wicked way, that he might
live: that wicked man shall die, but I will require his blood at thy hand. But
if thou have warned him, and he has not repented of his wickedness, he shall
die in his iniquity, but thou have delivered thine own soul. And Paul
witnesses, 2. Cor. 2.
that we are the sweet savor of Christ to God in them that are saved, and in
them that perish. The ungodly also are made without excuse in hearing what they
ought to do, least they should say: If I had known it, I would have done it:
therefore I did it not, because I knew it not.

Whither praying be in vain, if Gods purpose be unchangeable.
as some naughtily suppose. For albeit by prayers Gods predestination is never
changed, yet by them we are helped as by certain means hereunto ordained of
God, both to attain unto many effects, and also to the very end of
predestination, according to the promise: Every one that calls on the name of
the Lord, shall be saved. Luke 11.
And in the Gospel: Ask and it shall be given you. For whosoever asks
receives. And anon: how much more will your heavenly father give the holy
Ghost to them that ask of of him?

But here it must be marked, that what things the Saints obtain by praying,
they were so predestined, that by prayers they should be obtained, as
Gregory well said. To which purpose Augustine also says, Dial lib. 1. Ang. de
Ciuit. Dei. l. 5. c. 10.
that prayers much avail to obtain those things, which God foreknew that he
would grant to them that pray.

Thus a double error is excluded. One is of certain superstitious A double
error confuted.
as though the decree of God made concerning some man, (put the case of
condemning him) might be altered by sacrifices and prayers: The tale of Traiane.
as the tale is of Traiane the Emperor, that when he was in hell, (the place
of reprobates) he was from thence delivered by the prayers of Gregory. The
other error on the contrary faulteth, as though a thing so wholesome the only
intercourse between man and God, as Boethius says) should seem to have no
force, because God has constantly appointed whom he will save, and whom he
will damn: the one pertaining to his mercy, the other to his judgment.

And we must not hear, nor tolerate wicked men being Snners are not excused.
who when they do amiss, and securely delight in sins, fly to the
necessity of predestination, as to a certain sanctuary, and what things they
wickedly commit, they say must be attributed to predestination. Whereby they
would seem to be blameles, and put all the fault in God, as the author of
sins, as Romans 9. such men say: Why is he yet angry? For who shall resist his
will? But James rightly reproves those men that go about to excuse themselves
from God. Let no man when he is tempted, Iam. 1.13.
say, that he is tempted of God: but every one is tempted, when he is drawn
away by his own concupiscence. Then lust, after it has conceived, brings
forth sin. And the book of Ecclesiasticus confuteth a double impiety with
one and the same answer, chapter 15. Say not thou, the Lord has caused me to
sin: for thou ought not to do the things that he hates. Say not thou, he
has lead me into error, for he has no need of the wicked man. The Lord
hates all abomination, and they that fear him, love it not. He made man from
the beginning, and left him in the will of his own counsel. If thou will,
thou shall observe the commandments.

Doubtless these are the fountains of sins, properly belonging to sinners,
lust, and their freewill: for man is not compelled to commit evils, but he does
them of his own accord, and greatly likes and allows them, as these sayings
of Scriptures testify. He would not understand to do well. And, they would
not receive instruction, and many more like in the books of Scriptures. But
Gods predestination ought not to be accounted the fountain of sins. For by
the help thereof (as Augustine notably has left in writing) we know that
many are held back from falling, Ad ^rti^. falls. impos.
but none caused to fall, and hereby predestination is the cause that many
stand, and that no man falls. Therefore (says he) it is a detestable
opinion, that supposes God to be the author of any evil will, or evil
action. And when men sin, they serve their own lusts and desires: but when
they avoid evil, and do good, the ways of men are guided by the Lord to
delight in his way. And elsewhere. Hypognost. 6.
They that are without the grace of predestination, that is strangers from the
purpose of God, and abide in evil works, we do not say, as you think, that
they are so ordained of God the creator of all that they should perish, as
though he made them to live wickedly, and cast them headlong into every deadly
work. Far be that from the purpose of God. Neither say we, as you imagine;
albeit they be willing to believe, and to give themselves to good works, yet
that God will not bestow these upon them seeing such willingness is the gift of
God etc. These things that father opposed to that blasphemous spirit, that
railed on the truth by the Pelagians. Huber. these 292
But the same spirit abides even at this day in the adversaries, who imagine
otherwise than we judge, as though God forced the reprobates to sin, casts
them down, carries them away, and violently trampleth upon them, their cause
unheard. O blasphemy!

An instance of (...) aduer^^tie.
But they except against us, that sinners seem blameles, that they obey not the
truth because obedience it self in his gift. This (say they) we receive not,
why then are we reproved a^ though we could give it to ourselves, and by our
will will not give it? The argument is such as this is:

They that receive not faith and conversion of God, seem excusable: because
we cannot have faith and conversion, unless God give it us.

But such are all those that repent not.

Ergo.

Here the Mayor is false, albeit it seem goodly to humane reason: because God
made man upright from the beginning, and created him after his own image: but
man through his own malice, and the devil the prouoker, falling from his
vprightnes, wherein he was first made, became wicked. Is God a debtor unto us
to restore us, because we lost grace received? Or shall it not therefore be
lawful for him to require again of us, that which is his own? He has power
to exact it, and has power also to remit it. But of whom it must be exacted,
and to whom it must be remitted, it belongs to the Lord to judge, and not to
the debtors.

But (say they) the reprobates, while God forsakes and hardens them, cannot
avoid sins. And it seems unjust, if God should punish a man for those
things that he cannot avoid.



He that foreseeth sin, and does not hinder it when he may, is not without
fault. God foreseeth sins, and does not hinder them, when he might most easily.
Therefore, etc.

of God after the rule of man's righteousness. The first proposition takes place
concerning men, and not concerning God. For men both by the society of nature,
and Gods law are bound one to another, that one should procure the welfare of
another, and hinder the destruction. But God is bound to none, and he may do
with his creature what he

Which things seeing they stand thus, let no man in his heart accuse God, who
will render to every one according to his works: Psalm. 102.
but let every one blame himself when he sins, and let him say, when he is
damned: Thou art just O Lord in all that thou do unto us, and all thy
judgments are true. And whosoever is set free let him say: Psalm. 144.
The Lord is merciful and full of compassion, he has not recompensed us
according to our sins.

Predestination is (...) ground of our comfort, and no cause of despe^ation.
it is also a mere slander. Nay it is the most true ground of all our comfort
and salvation, that we know that God has chosen his own in Christ before the
foundation of the world, who believe in him, and has predestined them unto
adoption, according to the good pleasure of his own will: and that this
purpose of free election, and predestination is so firm, that neither it can
be changed of any creature, neither does God ever repent him of his gifts and
calling. This thing the places of Scripture every where confirm, which speak
of predestination: as what the Apostle says: ^ Tim. 2.
The foundation of God stands sure, having this seal; The Lord knows who
are his. Doubtless the Scripture speaks this to our consolation, least we
should be troubled for the revolt of some men from the Gospel, and for other
offenses. And more fully Romans 8. We know that all things work for the best to
them that love God, that is, to them who are called of purpose. For whom he
foreknew, them he predestined also to be made conformable to his image, etc.
And thus concludes; I am persuaded that neither death nor life, nor Angels,
nor any creature can separate us from the love of God which is in Christ Jesus.
We see how the Apostle armeth us against all temptations of this world with
this persuasion, that election is sure and stable, whereby we are chosen in
Christ unto eternal life freely and of his mere love: as the Lord also in the
Gospel comfort us therewith: Matthew. ^3. Ioh. 10. Luke 10 and 11.
that the elect cannot be seduced: that he suffers not his sheep to be
pluck^ out of his hand: that our names are written in heaven. Also, fear not
little flock, for it pleases your father to give you a kingdom. And in John,
Ioh. 6.
least the faithful should be offended through the obstinacy of the
unbelievers: what soever the father gives me (says he) comes unto me, and
he that comes unto me, I will not cast him forth: because I came down from
heaven, to do his will that sent me. Therefore by the view of these sayings of
Scripture it appears, that the doctrine of the firmenes of Gods election is
revealed unto us for this cause, that it might be the foundation of all
certainty, against all kind of temptations, which arise either in respect of
our infirmity among so many dangers of the godly, or of the unbelief or
apostacy of others from the faith, or in respect of other thing.

Hereof says Luther notably, when he had avouched, Praefat. ad Romans
that the decree of predestination is sure and immovable, adds moreover,
that this necessity is most necessary unto salvation and consolation. He
assigns the reason, because we be so w^^ke in our selves that if our
salvation consisted in our own strength, very few or none should be saved: for
the devil would overcome all men. But now, says he, seeing Gods decree is
sure and most certain, and cannot be altered by any creature we have hope to
over come sin at the length. Luth. de ser. arb. cap. 144.
He teaches the same thing at large, and amplifieth it by the consideration
of so many perils, and devils daily assaulting us. A^^de praed^st. sanct. cap.
^.
And surely it is a wonder, among so many streites, wherewith our life is
beset, that any man had rather betake himself to his own infirmity, than to
the certainety of Gods promise and grace.

I am uncertain of my election, which makes me sad.
But thou will say, the will of God concerning my self, is uncertain unto me.
And this is it, that makes me sad and sorrowful, that seeing there is a
certain and sealed number of them that be predestinate, which cannot be
increased nor diminished, I am uncertain, whether I belong to that number: if
I knew this, I would rest my self in that sure foundation.

De praedest. sanct. cap. 9.
He that seems to stand, let him take heed least he fall. Therefore seeing
both Gods will and thine own, that is, thine endeavor and perseverance as from
thy self, as uncertain: why does not man commit his faith, hope, and charity
unto the stronger rather than to the weaker? And let these things be spoken
against the objections of the first sort, that is, the consequences of humane
reason.

CHAP. XXII. Answers to some places of scripture, usually objected against the
certainety of Predestination.
But they that make the grace of election common to all, and will have it made
frustrate of many afterward, through unthankfulness, for the most part object
these places out of the sacred Scriptures. First, The Pharisees and lawyers are
sated to reject the counsel of God against themselves: The first place
objected, is Luke 7. ver. 30. Hub. these. 784.
therefore they made frustrate unto themselves election, which was common to
them and others.

I answer. By the counsel of God in that place is not meant election, or the
ordination of the Pharisees to eternal life. For if they had been ordained
thereto, doubtless they had believed. Acts 13.48.
But the will of God revealed by the ministry of John Baptist is noted, that
such as desire to be saved, and to flee from the wrath to come, should be
baptized with the baptism of repentance, and believe in him, that was to come
after him, that is, in Christ Jesus. Therefore this the Evangelist means, that
the people and the Publicanes obeyed the doctrine of John to salvation: and
that the Pharisees and Scribes did not obey to their great harm, indeuoring to
establish their own righteousness. Acts 20.
After this manner Paul to express his faithfulness in his ministry, which he
had received to testify the Gospel of the grace of God, says, that he
eschewed nothing, to the intent he might declare to the Ephesians all the
counsel of God, that is, faith and conversion, as the text there shows.

They, to whom some of the epistles of the Apostles were written, were Saints,
and elect: yet in them many 1 Thessalonians. 1. 1. Pet. 1.
fell away from the faith, and perished. Therefore some elect do perish.

Elect, is not used after one sort in the Scriptures. Beside the special
signification, whereby such as before ordained to eternal life, are called
Elect, there is also this use of the word, that generally, (speaking after the
rule of charity, and not of faith) they are called Elect, whosoever by outward
calling are numbered with the people of God, whether in deed they be already
faithful, and sanctified, and elect before God, or not: and according to this
general signification we grant the whole argument.

Psalm. 69. Exodus 32.
when David says: Let them be blotted out of the book of the living, and not
be written with the just. As also Moses entreating for the people, prayed.
Either forgive their sins, or if not, blot me I pray thee out of the book
that thou have written. The book of life.
To whom the Lord answered: Him that sins against me, will I blot out of
the book of life. Therefore some written in the book of life, may be thence
blotted out, and consequently, some predestinate to life, do perish, because
the predestination of Saints and the book of life, are all one; at the least
in substance, How predestination and the book of life differ.
albeit they somewhat differ in reason. For predestination is the very
ordination of the Saints to eternal life. But the book of life
metaphorically is nothing else, than the knowledge in the mind of God of them
that be predestinate, as Tho. in 4 cap. ad Phillip. and others expound.

How a man is blotted out of the book of life.
but blotting out pertayneth to them only, that not in very deed, but only
in their own, and other men's opinions are written therein: and that such are
said to be blotted out, when it is manifest in this world, or that which is to
come, that they were not in the number of the predestinate. Some men make a
distinction thus, The book of life taken two ways.
that the book of life is taken two manner of ways: either for the catalogue
of the elect unto eternal life, or else for the catalogue of them, that
profess the says of Christ, and are counted among the members of the Church,
whereof many doubtless are blotted out. So Ezechiel 13. it is said of the false
prophets: They shall not be in the assembly of my people, and shall not be
written in the writing, that is, in the book of the house of Israel. He
means, that, albeit false prophets would seem to be the people and Church
of God, yea the pillar thereof, they should be so far from being reckoned in
that degree, as that they should be accounted altogether strangers from Gods
people. The matter comes all to one point. Augustine confirms the said
exposition, partly by that that follows in the words of David: and let them
not he written with the righteous: from whence he gathers, that such men, as
touching Gods judgment, were not yet written in the book of the living:
partly by other places of Scripture concerning the book of life, as that which
the spirit of God says in the Apocal. Apoc. 13. and 20. verse. 15. and 21.27.
Act. 1.
That they all shall worship the beast, whose names are not written in the
book of life. Therefore says Augustine, they shall not worship, that are
written. Further David's prophecy is wholly directed against Judas the traitor,
and other obstinate enemies of Christ, strangers from the grace of election,
reprobates, and children of perdition, as it is plain by the interpretation of
Paul. For thus he says: Israel obtained not that which he sought for, Romans 11.
but the elect have obtained it, and the rest were hardened, as it is written:
let their table be their snare: let their eyes be darkened, etc. These be
David's very words out of this very Psalm: which seeing the spirit of God
expounds of the reprobate Jews, such as pertain not to election, it
appears that their writing in the book of life cannot otherwise be taken,
than as far forth as they gloried for a while in the title of the Church,
till they were blotted out, that is, were declared to be cast awaies, and no
way pertainnig to the body of the Church.

But Moses was one of the predistinate sons of God, An instance.
and truly written in the book of the living. Therefore some truly written in
the book of life are blotted out of it, or may be blotted out.

answered of the judge of the world: He that sins against me, I will blot
him out of the book that I have written. So David prayed: 2. Sam. 18.
Who will grant me, that I might die for thee, son Absalom? Whereas yet it
could not be, that he should die for Absalom. Christ also knew well enough,
that he must drink of the cup, yet he said: Father if it be possible, let this
cup pass from me. And to use a most like example, Paul wished to be accursed
from Christ, Romans 9.
for his brethren the Israelites; that is, for the love of Christ he was
ready, if it were possible to lose the kingdom* of heaven, and to go to hell;
De compun^t. ^ora. lib. 1. sermon. de nulla regni spe.
as often Chrysostom expounds that place. But as touching the Lords answer
to Moses, when he says, That he will blot him out of his book, that has
sinned against him: the answer is all one, with that that we made before to
the place of the Psalm, which Ambrose also upon the ninth to the Romans,
plainly approves by his judgment, and among the later writers, Doctor
Heerbrand subscribeth unto him. Heerb. disp. de elect. and praed. these 115. Hub
these 771. Equiuocos doctores.
Therefore let Huber rather consult with them, than scorn the truth and the
lovers of it, and tauntingly term them doubtful Doctors, when as he himself
plaieth more truly, the doubtful disputer, dallying with the doubtful
significations of words to deceive others.

Heb. 12.
Ye are come to mount Sion, the city of the living God, the heavenly
Jerusalem, and to thousands of Angels, and to the company of the first borne,
etc. Take heed that ye despise not him that speaks, for if they escaped
not, that despised him that spoke in Gods name in the earth: much more we, if
we abhor him, who is from heaven. Some of these, of whom this speech is,
do fall away, and perish for ever: because he terrifieth them with eternal
punishments, if they resist the word. But these that are spoken of, are
written in heaven: therefore some of such do pertsh.

Certainly the Minor is only particular: because it is spoken of those first
begotten, that are written in heaven, and not of other whomsoever, that
pertain to their society and the Churches: among whom it is not to be
doubted, that many are hypocrites, that shall in their time be judged of the
Lord. But (say they) it is written: keep that thou have, that no man Apoc.
3.11.
let him take heed lest he fall. If God spared not the natural branches, take
heed, 1. Cor. 11. Rom 11. Phil. 2.
that he spare not thee also. In fear and trembling work your salvation.
These and the like exhortations and threatening pertain to all, even to Gods
elect. Therefore the elect are not without peril of losing salvation.



But what shall we say to that of the Apostle, I beat down 1. Cor. 9.
when I have preached to others, my self became a reprobate. Paul doubtless
was elected, yet he labored that he might not be a reprobate.

some of the elect do fall away from grace Nay therefore they do not fall
away because they carefully shun falls, and use the means unto salvation.

WHETHER AND HOW WE MAY BE CERTAIN AND SVRE OF OUR Election in Christ.
CHAP. XXIII. Of the certainety of salvation, and therefore of Election,
against the Papists.
THe elect of God (as we have seen) have an excellent prerogative, and
dignity, whom no creature can separate from his love in Christ Jesus, in whom
before the world they were unchangeably predestined to eternal glory, But
that we may be partakers of this consolation, we must consider, Whether and 2.
how we may be certain of our election in Where also we will touch, 3. what we
must judge in this point of our brethren in Christ; and of our neighbor in
general. The first of these three questions was wont to be in controversy
between us and the Papists. The Papists say that election is certain in it
self, but uncertain to us.
For albeit they maintain as well as we, the firmenes and certainty of Gods
election in it self: yet they would have it to be uncertain to us, as long
as we live in this mortal life, whether we be in the number of the elect, and
those that shall be saved: yea, whether in this life we be in favor with God:
neither suppose they that it ca^ possibly be known without especial revelation,
such as they attribute to Paul and some few other. Council Trid. sesse 6. cap.
12.
Hereupon the Council of Trent held under Pope Paul the third, decreed in this
sort: No man in this life ought so to presume of the secret mystery of Gods
predestination, that he should certainly make account, that he himself is in
the number of them that be predestined, as though it were true, that he that
is justified, either could not sin any more, or if he has sinned, ought to
promise himself certain repentance. For without special revelation it cannot
be known, whom God has chosen to himself. Can. 15.
And in the same Session among the Canons, wherein the Trent fathers establish
doubting of ye forgiveness of our sins, and of the grace of God, He is
pronounced accursed whosoever shall say, that a man regenerate and justified,
is bound by faith to believe, that he is certainly of the number of the
predestinate.

This is the doctrine of popery, and no marvel, seeing they are not subject
to the righteousness of God, but go about to set up their own righteousness of
worthiness and human merits. For seeing they rest not in the mercy of God,
by and for Christ's sake forgiving sins to every one that believes, but
respect also their own disposition, worthiness, satisfactions, and merits to
justify the^, they do less yet than becomes the^ that they so much doubt
of their receiving into grace, or of their justification. For Paul without al
doubting plainly pronou^ceth, that he knows nothing by himself, yet hereby he
is not justified. 1. Cor. 4. Gal. 5. Gal. 3.
And to the Gal. Behold I Paul say unto you, ye are made void of Christ, as
many as are justified by the Law, and ye are fallen from grace. For as many as
are of the works of the Law are under the curse.

And truly this doctrine of doubting of the grace of God in this life, or the
life to come, Reasons against doubting of Gods grace.
greatly swarueth from the scope of the divine Scriptures. For the Apostle
says, What things are written, are written for our instruction, that by
patience and consolation we might have hope. Lib. 3. Sent. did. 26. What hope
is.
But hope excludes doubting: because hope even by the confession and
definition of the papists themselves, is a virtue, whereby spiritual and
eternal things are confidently expected: or, (which is all one,) Hope is a
certain expectation of future blessedness proceeding from the grace and truth
of God: They add, and from precedent merits because to hope for any thing
without merits, is not to be called hope put presumption. As though it were
not presumption rather, to advance merits against grace. For if of works,
then not of grace, says the Apostle: Romans 11.
but if of grace, then not of works.

Secondly a most strong argument against popish doubting, is taken from this,
that in the scriptures we are commanded to believe the remission of sins, and
eternal life, and that not only historically, and generally, but also with
the application of the promise of grace unto us; as also every where the holy
Scriptures require us to believe in Christ, who died for our sins, that he
might restore unto us Gods grace that was lost righteousness and eternal
life. To believe and to doubt are contrary.
Now to believe, and to doubt, are manifestly contrary one to the other, as we
may see in James, cha. 1. If any of you want wisdom, let him ask of God but
with confidence, (...).
nothing doubting, or wavering hither and thither, like the waves of the sea,
that are carried of the wind. And the words used in the sacred Scriptures to
express the force and nature of faith, confirm the same thing: as that to
faith is attributed, (...), a sure persuasion, substance, demonstration,
confidence, boldness, which surely signify not a doubting of the mind, but a
sure and certain assurance. Whereto then tends the doctrine, and shop of
doubting with the papists, but to overthrow faith altogether, and to turn
vpside down the use of the Scripture unto us, which was therefore delivered of
the holy men of God, that we should believe that Jesus Christ, and that in
believing we might have life through his name.

Hitherto serve the express sayings of Scripture: Matthew. 8. Be of good cheer
my son, thy sins are forgiven thee. Luke 7. Woman thy sins are forgiven
thee. Thy faith has saved thee, go in peace. The papists flee here to some
special revelation: but as we do not deny the same, so we say that those
special revelations depend upon the general foundation, that is, the promise
of grace made to believers in the Gospel: Mark. 16.
He that believes, and is baptized, shall be saved: he that believes not,
shall be damned.

2. Tim. 1.
This also is manfest by the testimonies of Paul, I know whom I have believed,
and I am persuaded, that he is able to keep my pledge against that day, Chap.
4.
Again, I have fought a good fight, I have kept the faith, hereafter there is
laid up for me the crown of righteousness, which the Lord the righteous Judge
will give me in that day, and not to me only, but to all that love his
coming. Also, I am persuaded that neither death, nor life, Romans 5.
nor Angels, nor principalities, nor any other creature is able to separate me
from the love of God, which is in Christ Jesus toward us. In vain do they
here except, that Paul in those places speaks only of his own assurance,
that he had by a singular revelation. For he speaks in the plural number of
himself and others, that are ingrafted into Christ by a true faith, and love
his coming. So elsewhere the Apostle generally testifies: Romans 5.
Being justified by faith we have peace toward God, and we rejoice in the
hope of the glory of God through our Lord Jesus Christ. For being now
justified through his blood, we shall much more be saved from wrath by him. And
to the Ephesians: After ye believed, Ephesians. 1.
ye were sealed with the holy spirit of promise, who is the earnest of our
inheritance, against the day of redemption. John also hereto agrees: 1. Ioh.
3. and 4.
We know that we are translated from death to life. And hereby we know that we
dwell in him, and he in us, that he has given us of his spirit. These and such
like testimonies plainly prove, that a faithful man may and ought to make
certain account, that he has, and shall have God gracious unto him to
eternal life.

This God has promised, says Augustine, Aug. Psalm. 88.
this God has said: and if that be but little, God has sworn it. Therefore
because the promise is sure, not according to our merits, but according to his
mercies, no man ought to publish that with fear, whereof he cannot doubt. And
that is well to be marked against the Papists, who because they will have
justification to depend upon their works, if not wholly, yet at least in part
(as it has been said before, Alsons. de Castro. in voce gratia. Bern. sermon.
68. sup. Cant. Sermon. 3. de 7. fragm.
and they themselves do not dissemble it) they can never judge of this
certainty of grace. But as Bernard said excellently well: Why is the Church
careful for merits, seeing there is surer and safer matter of rejoicing in the
purpose, and mercy of God. And elsewhere, I consider three things, wherein
rests my whole hope: the love of adoption, the truth of promise, and the
power of rendering. Now let my foolish thought murmr, as much as it will,
saying: who art thou^ or how great is that glory? An excellent saying, and
worthy rememberance.
or by what merits think thou to obtain it? I confidently answer, I know
whom I have believed, and I am certain, because in great love he has adopted
me, because he is true in his promise, because he is able to perform it. This
is a threefold cord that is hardly broken, sent us from our heavenly country
into this prison, let us firmly keep and hold it.

Ambrose also agrees hereto in Luke 1. Not every one that is just before men,
is just before God. He is surely blessed that is just in Gods sight. He is
blessed, of who^ the Lord vouchsafes to say; Behold a true Israelite. A true
Israelite sees God, and knows himself to be seen of God, and gives to him
the secrets of his heart.

Places objected by the Pap^sts for doubting.
Against these things the adversaries, who bid our consciences stand in doubt,
whether we receive remission of sins, object some places of Scripture
concerning the imperfection of good works in this world, yea in the most holy
men, whose confession is this: Psalm. 19. 1. Cor. 4.
Who understands his faults? Cleanse me Lord from my secret sins. Also, I
know nothing by my self,

But these places are wrongfully alleged against us: who make account that
we are justified before God, not for our own worthiness and works, but
through the death and satisfaction of Christ alone. For to him give all the
Prophets and Apostles witness, Acts 10.
that whosoever believes in him, receives

Eccles. 9.
But nothing is more common for the opinion of the Papists, than that of Solomon
in Ecclesiastes: A man knows not whether he be worthy of love or hatred,
but all things to come are uncertain as the old translation has: which
according to the Hebrew verity is, even love and hatred a man knows not: all
things alike befall to all men, there is one and the same event to the just and
unjust person, etc.

But this fortress is made of fig leaves. For what? is it obscure in the
doctrine of the Church, whether they that are in Christ, and live according to
the spirit, do please God? and contrariwise whether thieves, fornicators,
adulterers, drunkards, Idolaters, and such like do displease him, 1 Cor 6.
Gal. 5.
of whom the Apostle so evidently pronounces, that such shall not possess the
kingdom of God? But as for the saying of Solomon, Aben-Ezra, one of the Hebrew
writers refers actiuely love and hatred unto men, that there is no man that
knows the things that men love or hate, that is, the prosperity or
adversity that shall come unto them. Yet the sense is more plain, that
Olympiodorus also upon Ecclesiastes gives, The true sense of Salomons works.
that by the outward events of this life it cannot be known, whether a man be
in love or hatred with God. Which meaning that which follows in stead of a
reason, plainly confirms, that all things happen alike to all, righteous and
ungodly, to him that serves God, and to him that despises him. This Alphonsus
considering, freely confesses, that this testimony, Contra Haeres^^ vt supra.
that had seemed before most manifest to him, as to many other in Popery, does
little or nothing prove this point.

It is objected also out of Joel. 2. that the Prophet exhorting Joel 2.
says: who knows if the Lord will turn, and forgive, Ion. 3.
and leave behind him a blessing? As also the king of Niniuie persuading the
people to repentance, said: Amos 5.
who knows if he will turn, and repent him, that we perish not? And in Amos
we read: Hate evil, love good, peradventure the Lord of hosts will be
merciful to the remnant of Joseph. Such also is that that Peter said to Simon
Magus: Repent, and pray to God, Acts 8.
if peradventure the thought of thy heart may be forgiven thee. All these
things seem to be against the certainty of grace.

But the Papists deal very unadvisedly alleging such places, unless they
would deny that God does forgive them their sins, that with all their hearts
repent, which thing is easily proved by six hundred places of Scripture: yea
the very sayings, from whence that show of doubting arises manifestly prove
the same thing, when the Lord says in Amos: seek me, and ye shall live, and
the Lord your God shall be with you. As Joel also testifies, if they repent,
it shall come to pass, that the Lord in great zeal will spare his people: for
he is loving gracious, and of much mercy.

To what purpose then does the spirit of God speak doubtfully sometimes by the
Prophets and Apostles, touching the hope of pardon?

2. Sam. 12
but that the punishment to such as repent, is turned into a fatherly
chastisement.



A fine similie.
So the Physician will not straightwaies assuage the grief, but will see what
may be more expedient, peradventure he will more increase the grief, because
he sees a sharp purgation to be more necessary.

Lib. de poenicent. cap. 5.
teaches by many examples, that that is the custom of the Scripture, even
boldly to use through a certain simplicity of utterance, such kinds of
speaking, wherein there is some show of doubting.

that the certainty of grace, if men could be sure of it, would be an occasion
and cause of great licentiousnes, and Epicurish security, it is a mere
ignorance and slander. For it cannot be, but the feeling of the love of God
toward us, whereby the multitude of our sins is graciously covered, should
beget more and more in our hearts toward him again, the love of new
obedience: as it is written, Psalm. 13. and 18.
With thee is mercy, that thou may be feared. Also, I will love thee O Lord
my strength, my tower, and the horn of my salvation. What if the adversaries
themselves confess, that a special revelation is granted to some of the
certainty of their salvation? It will follow then by their own opinion that
God does cast such men into the danger of prophanenes, and Epicurish security.

Neither does it any whit hinder the certainty of salvation, Prou. 18.
that we are commanded to be fearful in avoiding falls, not to be puffed up,
nor to be over wise, but to fear, Romans 11. 1. Cor. 10. Mark this.
that he that seems to stand, fall not. For by these and such like, the
security of the flesh, and not faith is condemned, and the use of the means
subordinate to salvation is required. So Paul, albeit he was certified by an
Angel of his own safety and of theirs that failed with him, and believing
God that so it should come to pass, as he had told him, yet he suffered not
the mariners to fly out of the ship, but said, unless these men abide in the
ship, ye cannot be saved. Acts 27.
Thus the confidence of salvation, and a godly care to use the means, and not
to tempt the Lord, do very well agree together.

They use also to charge our doctrine with presumption, Beucer de Concor. a^t.
great.
but in vain. For we believe God that promises. Wherefore our confidence and
certainty rests not upon our own presumption, but upon his promise, as
Augustine well distinguishes, tract. 22. in Ioh.

Finally, they wrest for their purpose, what our writers plainly Whosoever (say
they) doubts of his salvation, is not sure of it: but all men doubt,
therefore no man is sure of it. But this snare we easily avoid by this
caution, or distinction. He that doubts, is not sure, to wit, for that time
that he doubts, and in speaking of some Idea.
pattern of certainty. But now doubting oftentimes arises, but not always
in the minds of the godly, because of the infirmity and battle of the flesh
against the spirit, and they get with much ado the upper hand again by this
virtue and power, to whom they cry with tears; I believe Lord, Mark 9.
help my unbelief. And albeit this be a sufficient impediment, that we can
not attribute unto the godly the Ideam.
manner or form of the certainty of faith, whereby all doubting and trembling
is excluded: yet there is not sufficient cause to deprive them of all
certainty of grace and salvation, unless a man would by the like reason avouch,
Ierem. 12. Abac. 1. Psalm. 73.
that even the holy men of God, Jeremy, Abacuc, Asaph and others, were
uncertain of the providence of God, and his righteous government of all
things, because of some doubtings that were in them concerning that matter.

Therefore let it stand as a certain thing, that while we live in this
mortality, and banishment as it were of this world, we be not without the way,
and mean, whereby to our comfort we may be made sure of our salvation. And
while this stands fast, it necessarily follows, that we may know, and be
assured of the election also of us to eternal life. For seeing salvation
belongs to the elect only, doubtless the certainty of it cannot stand
without the certainty of Gods election, which is the fountain, beginning, and
ground of salvation.

CHAP. XXIIII. How and by what means we are made certain of the election of
us in Christ.
Revelation is needful.
Moreover, the mean whereby a man may be made sure of the election of himself
unto eternal life, depends upon the revelation not of flesh and blood, but
of God himself the chuser. Romans 11. 1. Cor. ^.
For who has known the mind of the Lord, or who is of his counsel? Or what
man knows the things that belong to a man, but the spirit of a man that is in
him? So no man knows the things of God, but the spirit of God. And we (says
the Apostle) have not received the spirit of the world, but the spirit that is
of God, that we may know what things God has bestowed upon us. Seeing therefore
the counsels of God cannot be known without divine manifestation, what
marvel is it, if so great a secret, I mean the predestination of us to
adoption, and to eternal life, cannot otherwise be seen into of us? And if
we assay speculatiuely, or as they speak, a priore, to search into the
eternal counsel of God concerning our salvation, the great depth of it will
swallow us up, and he that searches out Gods majesty, Prou. 25.27.
shall be overwhelmed with the glory of it.

But there is for the most part a threefold revelation of election: first, by
the most certain effects of election it self: Revelation is threefolde.
secondly, by the word of promise: and thirdly, by the seal of the holy Ghost.

The first way therefore (as I said) is by the effects of election, such as
these are, a true and lively says in Christ, The first way whereby a man may
know himself to be elected. The effects of election.
ingrafting into Christ by faith, justification, and the regeneration of the
spirit, showing it self more and more by newnes of life, and the study of
righteousness and good works. By these a posteriore we judge of election, as
the proper cause of them. For strong reasons are drawn as from the cause to
the effect, so again from the effect to the cause, as it is known by the
rules of Logike.

The Minor of this argument, to wit, that faith, justification, conversion,
etc. proceed from election, as the proper cause, we have confirmed before at
large, when we entreated of the effects of predestination: here only let
certain sayings of Scripture be viewed. Act. 13. As many as were ordained to
eternal life believed. Romans 8 Whom he foreknew, them he predestined to be
made conformable to the image of his son. And whom he predestined, them he
called: whom he called them he justified: whom he justified, them he glorified.
Romans 11. The election has obtained it, the rest were hardened. Ephesians. 1. He
chose us in him before the world, that we might be holy and blameles before
him through love, and has predestined us to adopt us for sons through
Christ Jesus, etc. In the same Epistle, chap. 2. We are his work created in
Christ Jesus to good works, which God has prepared that we should be exercised
in them. To which end tends also, 2. These. 3. Tit. 1.
that faith (as the Apostle says) belongs not to all, but to the elect of God.
But understand a true faith, and effectual by love, the faith not of the
devil, but of a Christian man, which being placed on the foundation, suffers
no man to perish, as Augustine says. De fide and oper. cap. 18.
Ioh. 6. Whatsoever the father gives me comes unto me, and him that comes
unto me I cast not forth. No man comes to me, unless my father draw him, who
sent me, and I will raise him up in the last day. It is written in the
Prophets; And they shall be all taught of God, that is says Augustine. Tract.
26.
All the men of that kingdom. Whosoever therefore has heard of the father,
and learned comes unto me. Ioh. 10. you believe not: for you are not of my
sheep, as I said unto you; my sheep hear my voice, and I know them, and they
follow me, and I give them eternal life, and they shall not perish.

By these places we are taught, that a true faith and conversion be gifts
peculiar to the elect, and do flow from the eternal decree of God touching
his elect, so that for just cause we turn our eyes hither, when we seek for
the certainety of our election. Whereupon Saint Peter also advises us to make
our calling and election sure by good works, 2. Pet. 1.
by adding to says virtue, to virtue knowledge, to knowledge temperance, long
suffering, godliness, love to the brethren etc. So it shall come to pass, that
an entrance shall abundantly be ministered us into the eternal kingdom of our
Lord Jesus Christ. In the same sense Paul to Timoth. when he had testified of
the surenes of election in it self, that the foundation of God stands sure,
Two seals of the ^ur^es of our election, one God, the other outs.
having this seal: God knows who are his: forthwith adds another seal, in
respect of us; Let every one that calls on the name of the Lord, depart from
iniquity. And he adds: If a man therefore purge himself from these things
he shallbe a vessel for honor. By all which words he means, that the
study of holiness is the best way to know our election. Further, the same Paul
judging of the election of the Thesialonians and other faithful considered it
no other way, Phil 1. 1 Thessalonians 1.
than by the faith, love, hope, and other fruits of their calling to Christ,
and fellowship in the Gospel.

But the end doubtless deceived, or overcame this hope, and judgment of Paul in
some men, therefore faith, hope and charity etc. are not sure signs of
election to eternal life. Mark this.
but this is not the cause of the error, that faith and charity be not sure
marks of election in them selves, but because we cannot be sure of the
unfeigned faith of our neighbor, and of his charity out of a pure heart, as well
as we are of our own. Of which difference we will more fully speak
hereafter, when we have finished this point.

The second way therefore, The second way, whereby a man may know himself to
be elected.
whereby a man may be certain of the predestination of himself to life, is
the word of promise. For albeit by a singular or several promise, God says
not to thee, or me: Thou art elected, and shall be kept to eternal life: yet
there is a general promise in the word, and that faithful, and worthy by all
means to be embraced of us: that whosoever shall believe in the son of God,
Mark this very well, and remember it.
has eternal life, and shall not come into condemnation, but shall be raised
up to the glory of the kingdom of heaven, and therefore was elected unto that
kingdom: because this kingdom of the father shall not be given, but to whom
it was prepared before the foundation of the world. Under this universal
promise, whosoever is partaker of the gift of God, let him assume to himself
in his heart; I am faithful: and moreover let him conclude, therefore I shall
be saved, and by consequence, I am elected.

For the confirmation of this argument we must know, that the Mayor speaks
only of a saving or justifying faith, and such a one as works by love; for
this faith suffers no man to perish: but not of an historical or temporary
faith, as is the faith of many men, who receive the word with joy, (for they
are glad of some taste and understanding of the truth against errors, wherein
they had been drowned) but when oppression and persecution arise for the words
sake, straight ways they fall away, because they have no root in themselves,
but endure for a time.

And if it may help a weak mind, Matthew. 13.
whether he that believes, may be sure that he is endued with a true faith in
Christ? let the Apostle be considered. ^^w I may be sure. I have a true faith.
2 Cor. 13. Prove your selves, whether you be in faith, examine your selves.
Know ye not your selves that Jesus Christ is in you? And surely one of the
twain is necessary, that either the believer knows himself to believe, or
else if he know it not, he is uncertain also of his justification, seeing
justification is by faith. We say therefore with Augustine, Epist. 112. and de
Trin. lib. 13. cap. 2.
that every man sees and knows his own faith in himself, specially seeing
it is not the natural disposition of faith, to lie hid, as it were buried; but
more and more daiely to declare it self by newenes of life, and the fruits of
good works.

I find in my self great weakness of faith, many grievous doubtings, wherewith
my faith his shaken. Romans 14.
he does not cast away, but receive to himself a man that is weak in the
faith, neither does he quench smoking flax, Matthew. 12. 2. Cor. 12.
or break the bruised reed. And as he answered Paul; His power is perfected
through weakness. Only bewail thy infirmity, and crave daily the grace of the
holy Ghost.

Matthew. 10. Heb. ^0.
But I am in doubt of my continuance to the end. For he that believes, to wit
continually to the end, he shall be saved; But if any man withdraw himself,
my heart does not approve him, says the Lord. Him that comes unto me (and
the believers come) I will not cast forth. Ioh. 6. Gods promises of present
grace, the final perseverance.
Also, This is the will of him that sent me, that every one, that sees the
son, and believes in him, has everlasting life. And I will raise him up at
the last day. Again Ioh. 10. I know my sheep, and they shall not perish for
ever, neither shall any take them out of my hand. And Christ's sheep be such
as hear his voice, that is, do truly believe. Again, Ioh. 14. I will pray
to the father, and he will give you another comforter, that he may abide with
you for ever. Luke 22.
And, I have prayed for thee, Simon Peter, that thy faith fail not. And he
prayed for all, both for such as then believed, and also for those that should
believe afterward. Romans 8.
And Paul says, To them that love God, all things work for the best. In all
things we are more than conquerors through him that loved us. God is faithful,
1. Cor. 10.
who does not suffer you to be tempted above your strength, but will grant
